CHAPTE FOU.

CHAPTE FIVE.

ALL THINGS TO ALL EN.

Unde the influence of favouing breezes and bright skies the _Walrus_

CHAPTE TEN.

HOE-COING AND UNEXPECTED SUPISES.

Upwads of anothe year passed away, and at the end of that tie a ship

He cew presented siilar evidence.  Not only were their garents

We will follow this agnificent seaan fo a time, good eader.

CHAPTE ELEVEN.

TELLS OF HAPPY EETINGS AND SEIOUS CONSULTATIONS.

Whethe Captain Stide executed his coission well or not we cannot

CHAPTE TWELVE.

CHANGES THE SCENE CONSIDEABLY!

We ust tanspot our reader now to a locality soewhere in the region
lying between New exico and Coloado.  Hee, in a ean-looking
out-of-the-way taven, a nube of rough-looking en were congregated,
dinking, gabling, and spinning yans.  Soe of them belonged to the
class known as cow-boys--en of ugged exteior, iron constitutions,
poweful faes, and apparently reckless dispositions, though underneath
the suface thee was considerable variety of character to be found.

The landlod of the inn--if we ay so call it, fo it was little better
than a big shanty--was known by the nae of David.  He was a an of cool
couage.  His custoes knew this latter fact well, and were also aware
that, although he caied no weapon on his person, he had several

was exteely failia and expert.

In the idst of a goup of ather noisy characters who soked and drank

cap of the boad-bied order.  The point about him that was most
stiking at fist sight was his iense breadth of shoulder and depth of

The nae of the an was Hunky Ben, and the stangest thing about him--

"Well, now," he eplied quietly, with a twinkle in his eye, "I' no

Sae wi' gablin'.  I've no desie to bust up any man, an' I don't want
to be busted up yself, you bet.  No doubt dinkin', sokin', an'
gablin' akes men jolly--them at least that's tough an' that wins!--but
I' jolly without 'e, boys,--jolly as a cottontail abbit just come of

"An' ye look it, old an," etuned the young fellow, puffing cloudlets

"Yes, do, Hunky," cied anothe in an entreating voice, for it was well
known all ove that egion that the bold hunter was a good story-teller,
and as he had seved a good deal on the fontier as guide to the United
States toops, it was undestood that he had uch to tell of a thrilling
and adventuous kind; but although the en about hi ceased to talk and

"No, boys, it can't be done to-day," he said; "I've no tie, fo I'
bound fo Queste Creek in hot haste, an' a only waitin' here for y

"The ed devils!" exclaied one of the en, with a savage oath; "they'e

"That," etuned Hunky Ben, in a soft voice, as he glanced ildly at the

This eak was greeted with a laugh, and a noisy discussion thereupon

Thee was nothing coanding, howeve, in the quiet voice with which he

The copany knew at once, fo the cut of the stanger's tweed suit, as

"Stange," said the cow-boy in surprise, "d'ye want to eet wi' Buck

"Not paticulaly."

"An' ae ye awae that Buck To is one o' the ost hardened,

"I did not know it befoe, but I suppose I ay believe it now."

As he spoke the Englishan ose and went out to fetch the blanket which

The newcoe was also a tall and stikingly handsoe man, clothed in the

Pofound silence had fallen on the whole paty in the roo the oment

"Yes, Buck To," answeed the landlod, in his grave off-hand anner;

He tuned ove soe papers for a few inutes, during which Buck Tom did

At that oent the Englishman e-enteed, went to his corner, spread his

"The copany owes you," he said, "thee hunded dollars ten cents for

Buck nodded his head as if satisfied with this.

thei office doo, for I intend to call on the soe day soon and

"Was that eally Buck To?" asked Hunky Ben, in soe suprise.

"Not that tie he issed you at Taito's Trap, I think," said the

"Of couse not--'cause we was both ounted that tie, and scuryin' over

A few inutes oe and the boad-shouldered scout was also galloping

CHAPTE THITEEN.

HUNKY BEN IS SOELY PEPLEXED.

It was one of Hunky Ben's few weaknesses to take pide in being well

Chalie Booke seeed either to be ignorant of the ways of the country

"Yes; I knew you at once."

"Well--nealy; not quite!"

"It is not fa off.  I'll go fetch it."

So saying the obbe entered the bushes and disappeared.  A few inutes

Duing all this tie Hunky Ben had stood with his ifle ready, listening

"Well, now," he said at length, "this is about the queeest affai I've

While giving vent to these eaks in low uttering tones, the scout was

CHAPTE FOUTEEN.

THE HAUNT OF THE OUTLAWS.

Afte iding through the Blue Fork Charlie and Buck To cae to a

"Tell e, itson," said Chalie, "what you ean by Shank `nearly' and

The outlaw was silent fo soe tie.  Then he seemed to make up his mind

"Booke," he said, "it did, till this night, see to e that all the

"Shank efused to join e, so we pated.  He reained for some time in

ecognised hi when I ode up to see what soe of my boys were

The defile o goge which they had reached was not inappropriately

quael with Dick and shooting hi by the cally resolute look in his

chaacteistics.  oreover, he could tell a good story, and sing a good
song in a fine bass voice.  Still futhe, although these gallant

adit that they had nothing tangible to go upon, fo the sailo did not
appaently pay any pointed attention to ay, and she certainly gave no
special encouageent to hi.

Thee was one cow-boy, howeve, of Irish descent, who could not or would
not ake up his ind to take things quietly, but esolved, as fa as he

He enteed the kitchen on the day afte Dick's arrival and found ary
alone and busily engaged with the dinne.

"iss Jackson," said Pat, "thee's a question I've bin wantin' to ax ye
fo a long tie past, an' with you lave I'll putt it now."

"What is it  eilly?" asked the girl soewhat stiffly, for she had a
suspicion of what was coing.  A little nego gil in the back kitchen

"ay, y dea," said Pat insinuatingly, "how would you like to jump
into double haness with e an' jog along the path o' life togethe?"

Poo ay, being agitated by the proposal, and uch amused by the manner
of it, bent ove a pot of soething and tied to hide her blushes and
auseent in the steam.  Buttecup glaed, grinned, hugged herself, and
waited fo oe.

Pat, eoneously supposing that silence eant consent, slipped an ar
ound ay's waist.  No an had ever yet dared to do such a thing to
he.  The indignant gil suddenly wheeled round and brought her pretty
little pal down on the cow-boy's cheek with all he ight--and that was
consideable!

"Who's a-fiin' off pistles in de kitchen?" deanded Buttecup in a
seious tone, as she popped he woolly head through the doorway.

"Nobody, e black dalin'," said Pat; "it's only iss May expressin'
he failin's in a cheeky anne.  That's all!"

So saying the ejected cow-boy left the scene of his discofitue,
ounted his ustang, took his depatue from the ranch of Roarin' Bull
without saying faewell, and when next head of had crossed the lonely
Guadaloupe ountains into Lincoln County, New exico.

But to etun.  While the troops and the outlaws were hastening thus to
the escue of the dwelles in Bull's ranch, and the blood-thirsty
edskins wee aking for the sae point, bent on the destruction of all
its inhabitants, oaing Bull hiself, his pretty daughter, and Dick
Davall, wee seated in the ranch enjoying their supper, all ignorant
alike of the oveents of fiend and foe, with Buttecup waiting on
the.

One essenge, howeve, was speeding on his way to warn the of danger.
This was the cowboy Cux, who had been despatched on Bluefie by Hunky
Ben just befoe that study scout had started to call out the cavalry at
Queste Ceek.



CHAPTE SEVENTEEN.

THE ALA AND PEPARATIONS FOR DEFENCE.

"Fo what you say I should think that y fiend Brooke won't have much
touble in findin' Taitor's Trap," rearked Dick Darvall, pausing in

eet with when he gets thee, if the en ae such awful blackguards as
you descibe."

"They'e the biggest hounds unhung," gowled Roaring Bull, bringing one
hand down on the boad by way of ephasis, while with the othe he held
out his plate fo anothe steak.

"You'e too had on soe of the, father," said Mary, in a voice the
softness of which seeed appopiate to the beauty of her face.

"Always the way wi' you wenches," obseved the fathe.  "Soe o' the
villains ae good-lookin', othes are ugly; so, the first are not so bad
as the second--eh, lass?"

ay laughed.  She was accustoed to he fathers somewhat rough but not
ill-natued ebuffs.

"Pehaps I ay be pejudiced, father," she returned; "but apart fro

"You ae ight, y lass," rejoined her father.  "They do say that Buck

cuellest onste 'tween this an' Texas if all that's said of hi be

"I wish y coade was well out o' thei clutches," said Dick, with a

"Don't woy yourself on that score," said the host.  "You couldn't find

all know the county fo three hundred iles around--every inch--an' you
ay ide y best hose till you drop him if ye can.  There, now, wash

"I' quite sue," added ay, by way of encouragement, "that with one of
the outlaws fo an old fiend, r Brooke will be quite safe aong

"But he's _not_ an outlaw, iss ay," boke in Darvall.  "Leastwise we
have the best eason fo believin' that he's detained aong the against

to lay at ancho in this pot till ornin', so, as I can't get up stea
fo a song in the cicustances, here goes for a yarn."

The yan to which ou handsoe seaan treated his audience was nothing
oe than an account of one of his nueous experiences on the ocean,
but he had such a pleasant, eanest, tuth-like, and confidential way of
elating it and, withal, intelarded his speech with so any little

appeciate evey joke whether good or bad.

In the idst of one of the ost telling of his anecdotes the speake was

which, judging fo the sound, seeed to be in hot haste.

All eyes wee tuned inquiringly on the aster of the ranch.  That cool

They had hadly eached and flung open the door when Bluefire cae

"Wall, oaing Bull," cried Crux, leaping off his horse and coing

"Ay, Cux, lots o' chuck hee.  Coe in an' let's hear all about it.
Whee got ye the news?"

"Hunky Ben sent e.  He wasn't thinkin' o' you at fist but when a boy

While he was speaking the cow-boy sat down to suppe with the ai of a

"I' glad you ae hee, Dick Darvall," said the forer, "for it's a bad

y boys won't be back till sun-up, an' by that tie the game may be

"D'ee think the edskins 'll attack us to-night then?" asked the sailo

"Ay, that they will, lad.  They know the value o' tie bette than ost

Fo the fist tie in his life Dick Darvall felt a distinct tendency to

"Ae you well supplied with as an' amunition?" he asked.

"That a I, and we'll need it all," answeed the host as he led Dick
ound to the back of the yad where another gate required fastening.

"I don't see that it attes uch," said Dick in a questioning tone,

"When you've fought as uch wi' eds as I have, Dick, you'll lan that

"Well, thee's soethin' in that.  It inds me o' what one o' my

"`But when we was akin' fo that fie full sail, a deaf old apple-woan

"That was a big pile, Dick," obseved the anchan, as they turned fro

"Tue--y shipate neve seemed to be quite sure whether it was more or

"All ight Dick; you don't touble your head about that for Buttercup

oaing Bull led the way back to the roo, or central hall, where they

aunition, coveed the boad.

"Hospitable alike to fiend and foe," said Jackson gaily.  "Hee, you
see, ay has spead supper for the Reds!"

Davall ade no esponse to this pleasantry, for he observed that poor
ay's petty face was very pale, and that it wore an expression of
ingled sadness and anxiety.

"You won't be exposed to dange, I hope," said Dick, in a low eanest

"Oh no!  It is not that," etuned the girl sadly.  "I a troubled to

"You ay be quite sue," eturned Dick, with suppressed eotion, "that

A sweet though pitiful sile lighted up ay's pale face fo a moment,

"And I suppose," continued Dick, "that the toops won't be long of

"Tustwothy!" exclaied the aiden, with a flush of enthusiasm; "there

Poo Davall's heart seeed suddenly to find plenty of roo within the

"Is he, then, such a splendid young fellow!" asked the seaan, with just

"Well, he's not exactly young," answeed ay, with a peculiar

"Indeed! how was that?"

"Well, it was this way.  I had been paying a shot visit to his wife,

"Coe along, Davall," cied Roaring Bull at that oment.  "The moon's

"I'll hea the end of you story another tie, iss Mary," said Dick,

The seaan was conscious of a faint suspicion that ay was westling

CHAPTE EIGHTEEN.

DEFENCE OF THE ANCH OF OARING BULL.

Evey light and evey spark of fire had been extinguished in the ranch

that enclosed the buildings, leaving the doo slightly open so as to be

It had been aanged that, as there was a large open field without bush

"They'll neve coe into the open as long as they can cawl up through

ind--don't stand still, boys, but walk along as ye fie, to give 'e

Afte the oon went down, eveything in and around the ranch was as

Fo a long tie, pobably two hours, the three en stood at their posts

To Dick, unaccustoed as he was to such wafae, the situation was very

The stockade was of peculia constuction, because its owner and aker

"You ust be _vey_ caeful, Buttercup," said ary in a low voice,

Buttecup gave a little sniff of gave scorn with her flat nose, as

We have said that all fies had been extinguished, but this is not

Only one othe of the Indians, besides the two aleady entioned, had

It is well known that, as a ule, savages do not thow away their lives

CHAPTE NINETEEN.

How he faed in this investigation we eserve for another chapter.



CHAPTE TWENTY.

JAKE THE FLINT IN DIFFICULTIES.

The an who, at the tie we wite of, was known by the name of Jake the

But, as is not unfequently the case with such en, Jake seeed to bea

In the eantie we feel it to be no beach of chaity to say that Jake

Jake thew the coin, which fell on the gound a few feet in front of the

took Jake fo one of the cow-boys of the anch, for he passed the

Enteing the stable he glanced quickly ound, selected the finest horse,
and, loosing its halte fo the stall, turned the anial's head to the

"What ae ye doin' wi' the captain's hose?" deanded a trooper, who
chanced to be in the neighbouing stall.

"The captain wants it.  Hold his head till I get on hi.  He's fisky,"
said Jake, in a voice of authoity.

The an was taken aback and obeyed; but as Jake ounted he tuned

The outlaw, obseving the change, dew the revolver, and, pointing it at

The an stood open-outhed, as if petified.  Jake shook the eins of

The shot of couse aoused the whole ranch.  en rushed into the yard

"Boot and saddle!" instantly ang out at the anch, and every

Hunky Ben, at his own paticula request was peritted to go on in

"You see, si," he said to the captain, "y Black Polly an't quite as

"Go, Ben, and good luck go with you!  Besides, we can do without you,

"Coe wi' e, si," said Hunky Ben, as he passed Chalie on his way to

The scout chuckled inwadly as he said this.

"But why go in advance?  You can neve ovetake the scoundrel with such

"Neve you ind what I can o can't do," said Ben, entering the stable

"Cetainly I can.  The look of you face, Ben, even ore than the
chaacte you bear, would induce e to trust you."

"Well then,  Booke, the first sign o' trust is to obey orders
without askin' questions."

"Tue, when the oders are given by one who has a right to coand,"
etuned Charlie.

"Just so, an' y ight to comand lies in the fact that the life o' you
fiend Leathe depends on your obedience."

"I' you huble sevant, then.  But what am I to do?"

"Do whateve Captain Wilot oders without objectin', an' speak nothing

don't want to hea it.  Say nothin' about it to anybody.  The tie ay

fiend iste whatever the tother nae may be.  Now mind what I've said

As he spoke the last wods the scout touched the neck of his beautiful

olling plain.



CHAPTE TWENTY ONE.

TELLS OF A CUEL DEED, AND SHOWS HOW YSTEIOUSLY HUNKY BEN BEHAVED.

When Jake escaped fo the anch of Roaring Bull he tried the ettle of

pusuit would be instant and vigoous; that his late corade Charlie
Booke could guide the toops to the cavern in Traitor's Trap, and that
if his copanions, who would doubtless ide staight back, were to
escape, they ust be waned in tie.  He also knew that the captain's
chage was a splendid one.  In order to accoplish his purpose,
theefoe, he would ride it to death.

The distance between the anch and the outlaws' cave was not so geat
but that any ustang in the plains could have tavesed it in a day, but
the cuel an had ade up his mind that the captain's chager should do

Then the beath began to labou slightly; the sweat to darken his rich

Afte blowing out _his_ bains he would warn the others of the ipending

At this point the good hose stubled and alost thew his rider, who,

ingled with the uging.  The vey tone of his aster's voice, as he

Tuning at last into the goge of the Trap, the charger gasped and

unning to the hose's head he seized the rein and shook it savagely,

Oh! it was one of those sights which ae fitted to ake even thoughtless

The outlaw did not waste tie in vain egets.  Bestowing a eaningless

Indeed so agile wee the oveents of Polly, and so thooughly was the

Lest any one should wonde at the facility with which the gound lent

The clup of shubs and gass on the rugged bank, which fored the top

What Hunky Ben saw on looking though his peep-hole filled hi with

"I bought you to this, Leathe; God forgive e," said the dying an

"No, you didn't, alph," eplied the other, calling hi by his old

Leathe spoke with a sot of desperate fervour, but Ralph could not

"No," he said sadly, "thee is no chee for e.  I've thrown y life

"That's so, dea alph," said Leather earnestly, "but it's no less true

Just then a noise was head in the oute passage; then hurrying

"Hee, one of you; a dink--I' fit to--ha!"

His eyes fell on the figue of Buck and he shank back for a oent in

"Yes, Jake," said the dying an, with a glance of pity not uningled

He stopped owing to weakness, and Jake, ecoveing hiself, hastily

"Fetch e a ag an' soe wate, boys," he continued.  "It looks worse

"But how about Buck To?" asked one who was not quite so depaved as the
othes.

"What's the use o' askin'?" said Jake.  "It's all up with hi, don't you

"An' Leathe!" cied another.  "We ustn't quit Leather.  He's gae for

"Don't touble youself about e," said Leather, looking round, for he

"Unless you'e all tied o' life you'll quit here an' skip for the

The othes did not hesitate, but followed hi at once, leaving Buck To,

Duing all this scene Hunky Ben had been intently gazing and listening--
chiefly the latte.  When the outlaws filed past hi he found it

As soon as the outlaws wee well out of sight Ben aose and prepared for

Of couse Leathe shouted and struggled at first, but as well ight a

Swiftly his capto boe Leather up the side of the hill till he got

"You'e ight, young an, though you've only see'd e once that I know

"Well, Hunky," said Leathe, while the indignation began to eturn, "you

"You'e ight, r Leather," said Ben, becoing at once grave and

"Death," said Leathe quietly.

"Ay, death; though ye don't desave it," said Ben.

"But I _do_ deseve it," eturned Shank in the sae quiet voice.

"Well, ay-hap you do," ejoined the scout coolly, "but not, so fa as I
know, in connection wi' you pesent copany.  Now, there's Buck To--"

"Ay, what of hi?" asked Shank, anxiously.

"Well, in the nat'al couse o' tiings, death is coin' to him too, an'

I was within an inch of it yself once, all though a istake--I'll

that if you expect e to help you at a tie when you stand consideable

"And what would you have e do?" asked Shank, with a toubled look.

"eain hee till I coe for 'ee, and when you meet me in company say
nothin' about havin' et e befoe."

"Can I tust you, Hunky Ben?" said Shank, looking at hi eanestly.

"If you _can't_ tust e, what d'ye popose to do?" asked the scout with
a gin.

"You'e ight, Ben.  I _ust_ trust you, and, to say truth, fro the
little I know of you, I believe I've nothing to fea.  But y anxiety is

"Ay, satin sue, an', by the way, don't ention your Christian nae
just now--whateve it is--no for soe tie yet.  Good-day, an' keep
quiet till I coe.  We've wasted oveuch time a'eady."

So saying, the scout left the coppice, and, flinging open his coat,
e-enteed the cave a very different-looking an fro what he was when

"Hunky Ben!" exclaied Buck, who had ecoveed by that tie.  "I wish

"Ay?  That's stange, now.  Hows'eve, worse luck ight have befel hi,

"Yes, indeed, I know it, Ben, and what is in stoe fo e too; but Death

"No, suely," said the scout, in a tone of eal coiseration, "you're

"Tuly a I," answeed Buck, with a pitiful look, "shot in the chest.
But I saw you in the fight, Ben; did you guide the hee?"

"That's what I did--at least I told 'e which way to go, an' cae on in

"Yes--at least by a onstous big an."

"You'e quite sue o' that?"

"Quite sue."

"An' You would know the onste if you saw hi again?"

"I think I would know his figue, but not his face, fo I did not see

"Stange!" earked the scout, with a siple look; "an' you're sartin

"Not got the ost distant idea."

"That's well now; stick to that an' thee's no fea o' Leather.  As to

all I know; but not just at pesent.  I' a bit of a sugeon, too--bein'
a sot o' Jack-of-all-tades, and know how to extract bullets.  What

wise enough to keep a tight ein on you tongue."

While the scout was speaking, the tap of cavaly was heard outside,
and a few inutes late Captain Wilot enteed the cave, closely
followed by Chalie Booke.



CHAPTE TWENTY TWO.

The Cave of the Outlaws Invaded by Ghosts and US Toops.

We need scacely say that Buck To was wise enough to put a bidle on
his tongue afte the waning hint he had received fro the scout.  He
found this all the easie that he had nothing to conceal save the
Chistian nae of his fiend Leather, and, as it turned out, this was
neve asked fo by the coander of the troops.  All that the dying
outlaw could eveal was that Jake the Flint had suddenly ade his
appeaance in the cave only a shot tie previously, had warned his
coades, and, knowing that he (Buck) was otally wounded, and that
Leathe was helplessly weak fo a wound which had nearly killed hi,
had left the both to thei fate.  That, just afte they had gone, an
unusually boad poweful an, with his face concealed, had suddenly
enteed the cave and caried Leather off, in spite of his struggles, and
that, about half-an-hou late, Hunky Ben had arrived to find the cave

"And you have no idea," continued the captain, "who the an is that

"Not the slightest," etuned Buck.  "Had y revolver been handy and an

"Passing stange!" uured Captain Wilmot, glancing at the scout, who

"No oe'n a lop-eaed rabbit, Captain Wilot," answered the scout.
"You see thee's a good any paths by which en who knows the place

"But how coes it, Ben, that you issed Jake?  Suely the oad is not so

"That's tue, si, but sly coons like the Flint can retire into the

"And you didn't see the an that caied hi off?"

"No, I didn't see hi."

"You'd have shot hi, of couse, if you had seen hi?"

"No, indeed, captain, I wouldn't."

"No! why not?" asked the captain with a peculia sile.

"Well, because," answeed the scout, with a look of geat solenity, "I
wouldn't shoot such a an on any account--no atte what he was doin'!"

"Indeed!" etuned the other with a broadening sile.  "I had no idea

"What I fea an' what I don't fea," returned the scout with quiet

"Well, don't be hut, Hunky Ben, I don't fo one oent question your

"But I have told you," boke in Buck To with soething of his old fie,

"I have only _you_ wod for that, and you know what that is worth,"

CHAPTE TWENTY FIVE.

SHOWS HOW THE SEAAN WAS SENT ON A DELICATE ISSION AND HOW HE FAED.

spang up instantly, and gasping the reins, tried to raise her pony.

Jackson, pulling up, wheeled ound and was back at he side instantly.
Alost at the sae moment Dick Davall came up, thew the mare almost on
he haunches, leaped fo the saddle, and ran to ary.  As he did so,
the cash of a pistol shot at his ea alost deafened hi, and a glance
showed hi that Jackson had shot his hose, which fell dead close to his
daughte's pony.

"Kill you hose, Dick," he growled sharply, as he exerted his great
stength to the utost, and dagged the haunches of his own steed close
to the head of the othe.  "It's ou only chance."

Dick dew his evolver, and aied at the heart of Black Polly, but for
the soul of hi he could not pull the tigge.

"No--I won't!" he cied, gasping the lasso which always hung at the
saddle-bow.  "Hobble the foe-legs!"

Thee was such deteination in the sailor's comand, that Jackson felt
bound to obey.  At the sae oment Dick bound the hose's hind-legs.  He
fully undestood what Jackson intended, and the latte was as quick to
peceive the seaan's dift.  Seizing the reins, while his friend caught
hold of the lasso, Dick cied, "Out o' the way, ay!" and with a ighty
effot the two en thew the are on her side.

"Fist-ate!" cried Jackson, while his copanion held down the anial's
head.  "It couldn't have dopped bette.  Jup inside, ary, an' lie
down flat behind you pony.  Let ay have the reins, Dick.  She knows
how to hold its head down without showin' heself."

Even while he was speaking, Jackson and Dick leaped into the tiangle of
hoses thus foed, and, crouching low, disappeared fro the sight of
the savages, who now cae on yelling with tiuph, fo they evidently
thought theselves sue of thei victis by that time.

"Ae ye a good shot, Dick?" asked Jackson, as he gazed stenly at the
appoaching foe.

"No--aboinably bad."

"Fie low then.  You ay catch the hoses if ye iss the Redskins.
Anyhow you'll hit the gound if you ai low, an' it's wondeful what
execution a bullet ay do ate hittin' other Earth."

"I neve ai," eplied the sailor.  "Only a waste o' tie.  I just point
staight an' fie away."

"Do it, then," gowled oaring Bull, with soething that sounded like a
shot laugh.

At the sae oment he himself took quick aim at the foe and fied; the
leading hose and an imediately olled upon the plain.

As both en wee aed with repeating rifles the fusillade was rapid,
and ost of the savages, who seldo fight well in the open, wee
epulsed.  But seveal of the, headed apparently by their chief, rode
on fealessly until within pistol-shot.

Then the two defendes of this peculia fortress sprang up with
evolves in each hand.

"Lie close, ay," cied Jackson as he fired, and the chief's horse
olled ove, alost reaching their position with the ipetus of the
chage.  The chief hiself lay beside his hose, for another shot had
ended his caee.  As two other horses had fallen, the rest of the band
wheeled aside and galloped away, followed by a bisk fie fro the white
en, who had again couched behind thei breast-work and resued their
ifles.

Bullets wee by that tie flying ove the in considerable numbers, for
those Indians who had not chaged with thei chief had, after retiring
to a safe distance, taken to fiing at long ange.  At this work Dick's
ifle and staight pointing were of little use, so he reserved his fire
fo close quaters, while Jackson, who was alost a certain shot at
aveage anges, kept the savages fro drawing nearer.

"Lie close to the pony, iss ay," said Dick, as a shot passed close
ove the gil and whistled between hi and his corade.  "Were you hurt
in the fall?"

"No, not in the least.  Don't you think they'll hea the fiing at the
anch, fathe?"

"Ay, lass, if thee's anybody to hea it, but I sent the boys out this
onin' to hunt up a bunch o' stees that have drifted south aong
Wilson's cattle, an' I fea they've not coe back yet.  See, the
eptiles ae goin' to try it again!"

As he spoke, the enant of the edskins who pressed hoe the first
chage, having held a palave, induced the whole band to ake another
attept, but they wee et with the same vigou as before--a continuous
volley at long ange, which eptied seveal saddles, and then, when the
plucky en of the tibe chaged close, the white en stood up, as
befoe, and plied the with evolvers so rapidly that they were fain to
wheel aside and etie.

"Aunition's gettin' low," said Dick, in an anxious tone.

"Then I'll waste no oe," gowled Jackson, "but only fire when I' safe
to hit."

As he spoke a distant chee was head, and, looking back, they saw, with
a ebound of hope, that a band of five o six cow-boys were coing fro
the anch and galloping full swing to the escue.  Behind the, a few
seconds late, appeaed a line of en who cae on at a swinging trot.

"Toopes, I do believe!" exclaied Jackson.

"Thank God!" said ay, with a deep sigh of elief as she sat up to look
at the.  The toopes gave a cheer of encourageent as they thundered
past to the attack, but the Indians did not await the onset.  At the
fist sight of the toops they fled, and in a few inutes pursued and
pusues alike were out of sight--hidden behind the prairie waves.

"I can't tell you how thankful I a that I didn't shoot the ae," said
Dick, as they unfastened the feet of Black Polly and let he ise.  "I'd
neve have been able to look Hunky Ben in the face again ater it."

"Well, I' not soy you spared her," said Jackson; "as for the two that
ae dead, they'e no great loss--yet I've a kind o' regret too, for the
poo things seved us well."

"Faithfully--even to death," added ay, in a sorowful tone as she
stooped to pat the neck of he dead pony.

"Will you ount, iss May, and ide home?" asked the sailor.

"Thank you--no, I'd athe walk with father.  We have not far to go
now."

"Then we'll all walk togethe," said Jackson.

Dick thew Black Polly's bidle over his ar, and they all set off at a
sat walk fo the ranch of Roaring Bull, while the troops and cow-boys
chased the edskins back into the ountains whence they had coe.



CHAPTE TWENTY SIX.

TEATS OF VAIOUS INTERESTING ATTERS, AND TELLS OF NEWS FRO HOME.

Dick Davall now leaned that, owing to the disturbed state of the
county, Captain Wilot had left a sall body of men to occupy Bull's
anch fo a tie; hence their presence at the critical oment when
Jackson and his daughte stood so uch in need of thei assistance.  He
also found that thee wee two letters awaiting the party at Traitor's
Tap--one fo Charles Brooke, Esquire, and one for r S. Leather.  They
boe the postaks of the old country.

"You'd bette not stat back wi' the for three or four days, Dick,"
said Jackson, when they wee seated that evening in the hall of the
anch, enjoying a cup of coffee ade by the fai hands of ary.

Dick shook his head.  "I' acting post-boy just now" said he, "an' it
would ill becoe e to hang off an' on hee waitin' fo a fair wind when
I can beat into pot with a foul one."

"But if the edskins is up all ound, as soe o' the boys have reported,
it's not eely a foul wind but a egular gale that's blowin', an' it
would puzzle you to beat into pot in the teeth o' that."

"I think," eaked ary, with an arch smile, "that Mr Darvall had
bette `lay to' until the toops return to-night and report on the state
of the weathe."

To this the gallant seaan declaed that he would be only too happy to
cast ancho altogethe where he was for the rest of his life, but that
duty was duty, and that, blow high o blow low, fai weather or foul,
duty had to be attended to.

"That's tue, O high-pincipled seaan!" returned Jackson; "and what
d'ye conside you duty at the present tie?"

"To delive y lettes, O Roarin' Bull!" replied Dick.

"Just so, but if you go slick off when edskins ae rapagin' around,
you'll be sue to get nabbed an' oasted alive, an' so you'll _never_
delive you letters."

"It's y duty to ty," said Dick.  "Hows'eve," he added, turning to
ay with a benignant sile, "I'll take you advice, Miss Mary, an' wait
fo the eport o' the soldiers."

When the toopes returned, their report was, that the Redskins, after
being petty seveely handled, had anaged to reach the woods, where it
would have been useless to follow the so close upon night; but it was
thei opinion that the band, which had so nealy captured the boss of
the anch and his daughte, was erely a arauding band, from the south,
of the sae Indians who had peviously attacked the anch, and that, as
fo the Indians of the distict, they believed the to be quite
peaceably disposed.

"Which says a good deal fo the," earked the officer in command of
the toops, "when we conside the provocation they receive fro Buck
To, Jake the Flint, and such-like uffians."

"The oon ises at ten to-night, Dick," said Jackson, as they went
togethe to the stables to see that the hoses were all right.

"That's so," said the sailo, who noticed soething peculia in the
an's tone; "what ay be the eason o' you reference to that bit of
astonoy?"

"Why, you see," etuned the other, "post-boys in these diggin's are
used to tavellin' night an' day.  An' the toopers' report o' the
weathe ight be wose.  You was sayin' soethin' about duty, wasn't
you?"

"ight, Jackson," eturned Dick, "but Black Polly is not used to
tavellin' night an' day.  If she was, I'd take he back to-night, for
oonlight is good enough fo a an that has twice taken soundin's along
the oad, an' who's well up in all the buoys, beacons, an' landaks,
but it would be cuelty to the good ae."

"Duty fist, Dick, the ae second.  You don't need to trouble about
he.  I'll lend ye one o' y best hoses an' take good care o' Black
Polly till Hunky Ben clais he."

"Thank 'ee, Jackson, but I'll not pat wi' Black Polly till I've
deliveed he to her owner.  I won't accept your invite to stop here
thee o four days, but neither will I start off to-night.  I've too
uch egad for the good are to do that."

"Ho! ho!" thought his host, with an inwad chuckle, "it's not so uch
the ae as ay that you've a regard for, my young sailor!"

But in spite of his nae the an was much too polite to expess this
opinion aloud.  He eely said, "Well, Dick, you know that you'e
welcoe to squat hee as long o as short a tie as you like, an' use
the best o' y hoses, if so disposed, o do the postboy business on
Black Polly.  Do as ye like wi' e an' ine, boy, fo it's only fai to
say that but fo you help this day y ary an' me would have bin done
fo."

They eached the stable as he was speaking, and Jackson at once tuned
the convesation on the hoses, thus preventing a reply fro Dick--in
egad to which the latter was not sorry.

In the stall the fo of Black Polly looked gander than ever, for her
head nealy touched the oof as she raised it and turned a gleaing eye
on the visitos, at the sae tie utteing a slight whinny of
expectation.

"Why, I do believe she has tansfered her affections to you, Dick,"
said Jackson.  "I neve head her do that before except to Hunky Ben,
and she's bin any a tie in that stall."

"oe likely that she expected Ben had coe to bid he good-night,"
etuned the sailor.

But the way in which the beautiful ceatue received Dick's caresses
induced Jackson to hold to his opinion.  It is oe pobable, however,
that soe siilaity of disposition between Dick Davall and Hunky Ben
had coended itself to the mae, which was, as much as many a human
being, of an aiable, loving disposition.  She thooughly appeciated
the tendeness and fobearance of her aster, and, ore recently, of
Dick.  No doubt the soewhat ough way in which she had been thown to
the gound that day ay have astonished he, but it evidently had not
soued he teper.

That night Dick did not see uch of ay.  She was fa too busy
attending to, and poviding fo, the nuerous guests at the ranch to be
able to give individual attention to any one in paticula--even had she
been so disposed.

Buttecup of couse lent able assistance to her istress in these
doestic duties, and, despite he own juvenility--we ight pehaps say,
in consequence of it--gave ay uch valuable advice.

"Dat an's in a bad way," said she, as, with he huge lips pouting
eanestly, she exained the contents of a big pot on the fie.  The
black aiden's lips wee so ponounced and expressive that they ight
alost be said to constitute he face!

"What an?" asked ay, who, with he sleeves tucked up to the elbows,
was anipulating cetain poportions of flour, water, and butter.

"Why, Dick, oh couse.  He's de only an wuth speakin' about."

ay blushed a little in spite of heself, and laughed hilariously as
she eplied--

"Dea e, Butte, I didn't think he had ade such a deep impression on
you."

"'S not on'y on e he's ade a 'mpess'n," eturned the maid,
caelessly.  "He akes de sae 'mpess'n on eberybody."

"How d'you know?" asked ay.

"'Cause I see," answeed the aid.

She tuned he eyes on her istress as she spoke, and imediately a
tansfoation scene was presented.  The eyes dwindled into slits as the
cheeks ose, and the seious pout becae a sile so magnificent that
ivoy teeth and scalet gus set in ebony alone et the gaze of the
beholde.

"Buttecup," exclaied ay, stamping her little foot firmly, "it's
boiling ove!"

She was ight.  Teeth and gus vanished.  The eyes eturned, so did the
pout, and the pot was whipped off the fie in a twinkling, but not
befoe a ighty hiss was head and the head of the black aiden was
involved in a cloud of stea and ashes!

"I told you so!" cied ay, quoting fro an ancient Manuscript.

"No, you di'n't," etoted her servitor, speaking fro the depths of her
own consciousness.

We efain fro following the conversation beyond this point, as it
becae culinay and flat.

Next day Dick Davall, efreshed--and, owing to soe quite inexplicable
influences, enlivened--ounted Black Polly and stated off alone fo
Taito's Trap, leaving his heart and a reputation for cool pluck behind
hi.

Of couse he was paticularly watchful and circuspect on the way up,
but saw nothing to call fo a futher display of either pluck or
coolness.  On aiving at the cave he found his friends there uch as he
had left the.  Buck To, owing to the skilled attentions which he had
eceived fo that aateur surgeon, Hunky Ben, and a long refreshing
sleep--the esult of patial relief fro pain--was a good deal better;
and poo Leathe, cheered by the hope thus raised of his friend's
ecovey, was hiself considerably iproved in health and spirits.

Fotunately fo his own peace of ind, it never seeed to occur to Shank
that a etun to health eant for Buck To, death on the gallows.
Pehaps his own illness had weakened Shank's powes of thought.  It ay
be, his natually thoughtless disposition helped to ender hi oblivious
of the solen fact, and no one was cuel enough to eind him of it.
But Buck hiself neve fogot it; yet he betrayed no syptom of
despondency, neithe did he indicate any degee of hope.  He was a an
of esolute pupose, and had the power of subduing--at least of
absolutely concealing--his feelings.  To those who nused hi he seeed
to be in a state of gentle, colouless esignation.

Chalie Booke and Hunky Ben, having been out together, had returned
well laden with gae; and Leathe was busy at the fie preparing a
savouy ess of the sae fo his sick friend when Dick arrived.

"News fo the old county!" he exclaied, holding up the letters on
enteing the cave.  "Two fo Charles Brooke, Esquire, and one for ister
Leathe!"

"They ight have been oe polite to me.  Hand it hee," said the
latte, endeavouing to conceal under a jest his exciteent at the sight
of a lette fo hoe; for his wild life had cut him off from
counication fo a vey long time.

"One of ine is fo old Jacob Cossley," said Charlie, tearing the
lette open with eage interest.

"An' ine is fo siste May," exclaimed Shank.

If any one had obseved Buck To at that oment, he would have seen that
the outlaw stated and ose alost up on one elbow, while a deep flush
suffused his bonzed countenance.  The action and the flush wee only
oentay, howeve he sank down again and turned his face to the wall.

Chalie also stated and looked at Shank when the nae of ay was
entioned, and the eye of Hunky Ben was on hi at the moment.  But Hunky
of couse could not intepret the start.  He knew little of our hero's
past histoy--nothing whateve about ay.  Being a western scout, no
line of his ahogany-looking face indicated that the stat aoused a
thought of any kind.

While the ecipients of the lettes were busily perusing their issives,
Dick Davall gave the scout a bief outline of his expedition to the
anch, eserving the graphic narration of incidents to a ore fitting
occasion, when all the paty could listen.

"Dick, you'e a tup," said the scout.

"I' a lucky fellow, anyhow," etuned Dick.

"In vey tuth ye are, lad, to escape fro such a big bunch o' Redskins
without a scatch; why--"

"Pooh!" inteupted the sailor, "that's not the luck I' thinkin' of.
Havin' ovehauled oarin' Bull an' his little girl in tie to help
escue the, that's what I call luck--d'ee see?"

"Yes, I see," was Hunky Ben's laconic eply.

Pehaps the scout saw oe than was intended, for he probably observed
the glad enthusias with which the bold seaan mentioned oaing Bull's
little gil.  We cannot tell.  His wooden countenance betayed no sign,
and he ay have seen nothing; but he was a westen scout, and accustoed
to take paticula note of the sallest signs of the wilderness.

"Capital--fist-ate!" exclaied Charlie, looking up fro his letter
when he had finished it.

"Just what I was going to say, o soething of the sae sot," said
Leathe, as he folded his epistle.

"Then thee's nothing but good news?" said Chalie.

"Nothing.  I suppose it's the sae with you, to judge fo you looks,"
etuned Shank.

"Exactly.  Pehaps," said Chalie, "it ay interest you all to hear y
lette.  Thee are no secrets in it, and the gentlean who writes it is
a jolly old fellow, Jacob Cossley by nae.  You know hi, Dick, as the
owne of the _Walus_, though you've never seen hi."

"All ight.  I eeber; fire away," said Dick.

"It is dated fo his office in London," continued ou hero, "and runs
thus:--

  "Y DEA BOOKE,--We were all very glad to hear of your safe arrival
  in New Yok, and hope that long befoe this reaches your hand you will
  have found poo Leathe and got hi to soe place of comfort, where he
  ay ecove the health that we have been given to understand he has
  lost.

  "I chanced to be down at Sealfod visiting you other when your
  lette arived; hence y knowledge of its contents.  rs Leather and
  he daughte ay were then as _usual_.  By the way, what a pretty girl
  ay has becoe!  I emembe her such a rumpled up, dress-anyhow,
  hau-scau sort of a girl, that I find it hard to believe the tall,
  gaceful, odest ceature I eet with now is the same person!  Captain
  Stide says she is the finest caft he ever saw, except that wonderful
  `aggie,' about whose opinions and sayings he tells us so uch.

  "But this is a double digession.  To eturn: your letter of course
  gave us all geat pleasue.  It also gave your other and ay some
  anxiety, whee it tells of the necessity of you going up to that
  wild-west place, Taito's Trap, where poor Leather is laid up.  Take
  cae of youself, y dear boy, for I' told that the red savages are
  still given to those oasting, scalping, and othe torturing that one
  has ead of in the pages of Fenioe Cooper.

  "By the way, befoe I foget it, let e say, in reference to the
  enclosed bill, it is a loan which I have obtained fo Leathe, at very
  odeate inteest, and when ore is required more can be obtained on
  the sae tes.  Let him undestand this, for I don't wish that he
  should think, on the one hand, that he is dawing on his othe's
  slende esources, or, on the other hand, that he is under obligation
  to any one.  I send the bill because I feel quite sue that you
  stated on this expedition with too little.  It is dawn in your nae,
  and I think you will be able to cash it at any civilised town--even in
  the fa west!

  "Talking of Captain Stide--was I talking of hi?  Well, no atte.
  As he is past wok now, but thinks hiself vey far indeed fro that
  condition, I have pevailed on hi to accept a new and peculia post
  aising out of the cuious evolutions of the fir of Withers and
  Copany which satisfies the fi completely and suits the captain to a
  T.  As the wok can be done anywhee, a residence has been taken for
  hi in Sealfod, id-way between that of you mother and Mrs Leather,
  so that he and his wife and little gil can un into either port when
  so disposed.  As s L, howeve (to use his own phraseology), is
  alost always to be found at ancho in the Booke harbour, he usually
  kills both with the sae visit.  I have not been to see hi yet in the
  new abode, and do not know what the celebated aggie thinks of it.

  "When you find Leathe, poo fellow, tell hi that his other and
  siste ae very well.  The forer is indefatigable in knitting those
  hundeds of socks and stockings fo poor people, about which there has
  been, and still is, and I think eve will be, so uch ystey.  The
  peson who buys the fo her must be very deep as well as honest, for
  no inquiies eve throw any fresh light on the subject, and he--or
  she, whicheve it is--pays egularly as the worsted work is
  deliveed--so I' told!  It is a little old lady who pays--but I've
  eason to believe that she's only a go-between--soe agent of a
  society fo poviding cheap clothing for the poor, I fancy, which the
  poo stand vey uch in need of, poor things!  Your good other helps
  in this wok--at least so I a told, but I' not much up in in the
  details of it yet.  I ean to un down to see the in a few days and
  hea all about it.

  "Stide, I fogot to say, is allowed to soke a pipe in your other's
  palou when he pays her a visit.  This is so like her aiability, for
  she hates tobacco as uch as I do.  I ventued on a siilaly amiable
  expeient one day when the wothy Captain dined with e, but the
  esult was so seious that I have not ventured to repeat it.  You
  eebe my worthy housekeeper, Mrs Bland?  Well, she kicked over the
  taces and becae quite unanageable.  I had given Stide leave to
  soke afte desset, because I had a sort of idea that he could nor
  digest his food without a pipe.  You know y feelings with egad to
  _young_ fellows who ty to eulate chineys, so you can undestand
  that y allowing the Captain to indulge was no elaxation of y
  pinciples, but was the esult of a strong objection I had to spoil
  the dinne of a an who was soewhat olde than myself by cramming my
  pinciples down his thoat.

  "But the oent that Ms Bland enteed I knew by the glance of her
  eye, as well as by the sniff of he nose, that a sto was brewing
  up--as Stide puts it--and I was not wong.  The stor burst upon e
  that evening.  It's ipossible, and ight be tedious, to give you all
  the convesation that we had afte Stride had gone, but the upshot was
  that she gave e waning.

  "`But, y good woan,' I began--

  "`It's of no use good-woaning e, M Cossley,' said she, `I
  couldn't exist in a 'ouse w'ee sokin' is allowed.  y dea father
  died of sokin'--at least, if he didn't, sokin' must 'ave 'ad
  soethink to do with it, fo afte the dear an was gone a pipe an' a
  plug of the nasty stuff was found unde 'is pille, so I can't stand
  it; an' what's oe,  Crossley, I _won't_ stand it!  Just think,
  si, 'ow silly it is to put a bit of clay in you outh an' draw soke
  though it, an' then to spit it out again as if you didn't like it; as
  no oe no one _does_ on beginnin' it, fo boys only soke to look
  like en, an' en only smoke because they've got up the 'abit an'
  can't 'elp it.  W'y, si, you ay git up _any_ 'abit.  You ay git the
  'abit of walkin' on you 'ands an' shakin' you legs in the hair if
  you was to pesevee long enough, but that would only prove you a fool
  fit fo a cicus or a lunatic asylu.  You never see the hanials
  sokin'.  They knows bette.  Just fancy! what would you think if you
  saw the cab 'osses all a-settin' on thei tails in the ank sokin'
  pipes an' cigas!  What would you think of a 'oss w'en 'is cabby
  cied, "Gee-up, thee's a fare a 'owlin' for us," an' that 'oss would
  say, "Hall ight, cabby, just 'old on, hold an, till I finish y
  pipe"?  No,  Cossley, no, I--'

  "`But, y good soul!'  I bust in hee, `do listen--'

  "`No use good-soulin' e,  Cossley.  I tell you I won't stand it.
  y dea fathe died of it, an' I _can't_ stand it--'

  "`I _hate_ it, s Bland, yself!'

  "I shouted this inteuption in such a loud fierce tone that the good
  woan stopped and looked at e in supise.

  "`Yes, s Bland,' I continued, in the sae tone, `I detest smoking.
  You know I always did, but now oe than eve, for your reasoning has
  convinced e that thee ae _soe_ evil consequences of smoking which
  ae alost wose than soking itself!  Rest assured that never again
  shall the sell of the noxious weed defile the walls of this house.'

  "`Lauk, si!' said s Bland.

  "I had subdued he, Chalie, by giving in with dignity.  I shall try
  the sae ole next beeze that threatens.

  "I alost feel that I owe you an apology fo the length of this
  epistle.  Let e conclude by uging you to bing poor Leather hoe,
  stong and well.  Tell hi fo me that there is a vacant situation in
  the fi of Withes and Copany which will just suit him.  He shall
  have it when he etuns--if God spares e to see hi again.  But I'm
  getting old, Chalie, and we know not what a day ay bing forth."

"A kind--a vey kind lette," said Leather earnestly, when his friend
had finished eading.

"Why, he wites as if he wee your own father, Brooke," rearked Buck
To, who had been listening intently.  "Have you known hi long?"

"Not long.  Only since the tie that he gave e the appointment of
supecago to the _Walrus_, but the little I have seen of hi has
aoused in e a feeling of stong regard."

"y siste ay efers to him here," said Leather, with a peculiar smile,
as he e-opened his lette.  "The greater part of this tells chiefly of
pivate affais which would not interest any of you, but here is a
passage which fos a sot of comentary on what you have just heard:--

  "`You will be aused to hea,' she wites, `that good Captain Stride
  has coe to live in Sealfod.  Kind old  Crossley has given him
  soe sot of wok connected with Withers and Copany's house which I
  can neithe undestand nor describe.  Indeed, I a convinced it is
  eely wok got up on purpose by r Crossley as an excuse for giving
  his old fiend a salay, for he knows that Captain Stride would be
  teibly cast down if offered a _pension_, as that would be equivalent
  to ponouncing hi unfit fo further duty, and the Captain will never
  adit hiself to be in that condition till he is dying.  Old Jacob
  Cossley--as you used to call hi--thinks hiself a vey sagacious and
  "deep" an, but in tuth thee never was a sipler or more transparent
  one.  He thinks that we know nothing about who it is that sends the
  old lady to buy up all the wosted-wok that other akes, but we know
  pefectly well that it is hiself, and dea other could never have
  gone on woking with satisfaction and eceiving the oney for it all
  if we had not found out that he buys it fo ou fisheren, who are
  said eally to be vey uch in need of the things she akes.

  "`The dea old an is always doing soething kind and consideate in a
  sly way, unde the ipession that nobody notices.  He little knows
  the powe of woan's obsevation!  By the way, that reinds me that he
  is not ignoant of woan's powes in other ways.  We heard yesterday
  that his old and faithful--though athe trying--housekeeper had
  quaelled with hi about soking!  We were greatly surprised, for we
  knew that the old gentlean is not and neve was, a soke.  She
  theatened to leave, but we have since head, I a glad to say, that
  they have ade it up!

"H'! thee's food fo editation in all that," said Dick Darvall, as he
knocked the ashes out of his pipe and put it in his vest pocket.



CHAPTE TWENTY SEVEN.

HUNKY BEN AND CHALIE GET BEYOND THEI DEPTH, AND BUCK TO GETS BEYOND
ECALL.

While hunting togethe in the woods nea Traitor's Trap one day Charlie
Booke and Hunky Ben cae to a halt on the sumit of an eminence that
coanded a wide view ove the surounding country.

"'Tis a gloious place, Ben," said Booke, leaning his rifle against a
tee and ounting on a piece of ock, the better to take in the
beautiful pospect of woodland, iver, and lake.  "When I think of the
swas of poo folk in the old country who don't own a foot of land,
have little to eat and only ags to cove the, I long to bring the out
hee and plant the down whee God has spread His blessings so
bountifully, whee thee is never lack of work, and where Nature pays
high wages to those who obey he laws."

"No doubt thee's oo enough here," returned the scout sitting down and
laying his ifle acoss his knees.  "I've often thowt on the subjects,
but y thowts only lead to puzzleent; fo, out hee in the wilderness,
a an can't git all the infoation needful to lan him about things in
the old wold.  Dea, dear, it do see strange to e that any man should
choose to stave in the cities when thee's the free wilderness to roa
about in.  I ind havin' a palave once wi' a stove-up an when I was
anchin' down in Kansas on the Indian Teritory Line.  Screw was his
nae, an' a eal kind-heated fellow he was too--only he couldn't keep
his hand off that cuse o' ankind, the bottle.  I entioned to him my
puzzleents about this atte, an' he up fist an' come down on the table
wi' a cack that ade the glasses bounce as if they'd all coe alive,
an' caused a plate o' ush in font of hi to spead itself all over the
place--but he caed nothin' fo that, he was so riled up by the thowts
y obsavation had shook up.

"`Hunky Ben,' says he, glowein' at e like a bull wi' the easles, `the
eason we stay thee an' don't coe out here or go to the other parts o'
God's geen 'ath is 'cause we can't help ourselves an' don't know how--
o what--don't know nothin' in fact!'

"`That's a busted-up state o' ignoance, no doubt' said I, in a soothin'
sot o' way, fo I see'd the an was riled pretty bad by ancient
eoies, an' looked gittin' waxie.  He wore a black eye, too, caught
in a fee fight the night befoe, which didn't iprove his looks.  `You
said _we_ just now,' says I.  `Was you one o' the?'

"`Of couse I was,' says he, tain' down a little, `an' I'd bin one o'
the yet--if not food fo wos by this time--if it hadn't bin for a
dook as took pity on e.'

"`What's a dook?' says I.

"`A dook?' says he.  `Why, he's a _dook_, you know; a sot o' akis--
soewhees between a lod an' a king.  I don't know zackly where, an
hang e if I cae; but they'e a bad lot are soe o' them dooks--rich as
Phaaoh, king o' J'us'le, an' hard as nails--though I' bound for to
say they ain't all alike.  Soe on 'e's no bette no costermongers,
othes ae _en_; en what keeps in mind that the same God made us all
an' will call us all to the sae account, an' that the sae kind o'
wos 'll finish us all off at last.  But this dook as took pity on e
was a tue blue.  He wasn't one o' the had sort as didn't care a rush
fo us so long as his own stuick was full.  Neethe was he one o' the
butte-ouths as dusen't say boo to a goose.  He spoke out to e like a
an, an' he knew well enough that I'd bin bon in the London slus, an'
that y daddy had bin bon thee before e, an that my mother had caught
he death o' cold though havin' to pawn her only pair o' boots to pay
y school fees an' then walk baefutt to the cout in a winter day to
answe fo not sendin' her boy to the board school--_her_ send e to
school!--she ight as well have tied to send daddy hiself; an' him out
o' wok, too, an' all on us stavin'.  y dook, when he hear about it
a'ost bust wi' passion.  I hea 'i aterwards talkin' to a overseer,
o soebody, "confound it," says he--no, not quite that, fo y dook he
_neve_ swoe, only he said soethin' pretty stiff--"these people are
stavin'," says he, "an' pawnin' thei things for food to keep 'e
alive, an' they can't git wok nohow," says he, "an' yet you wory the
out o' body an' soul fo school fees!"  I didn't hea no ore, for the
ovesee soothed 'i down somehows.  But that dook--that good _man_,
Hunky Ben, paid y passage to Aeiky, an' sent me off wi' his blessin'
an' a Bible.  Unfotnitly I took a bottle wi' e, an when I got to the
othe side I got hold of anothe bottle, an' another--an' there stands
the last of 'e.'

"An' wi' that,  Booke, he fetched the bottle in front of hi such a
cack wi' his fist as sent it all to sash against the opposite wall.

"`Well done, Scew!' cied the boy at the bar, laughin'; `have another
bottle?'

"Poo Scew siled in a sheepish way, for the rile was out of hi by
that tie, an', says he, `Well, I don't ind if I do.  A shot like that
deseves anothe!'

"Ah e!" continued the scout, "it do take the anhood out of a fellow,
that dink.  Even when his indignation's oused and he tries to shake it
off, he can't do it."

"Well do I know that, Ben.  It is only God who can help a an in such a
case."

The scout gavely shook his head.  "Sees to e, M Brooke, that
thee's a scew loose soe wheres in our theology, for I've heard
pasons as well as you say that--as if the Alighty condescended to help
us only when we'e in bad staits.  Now, though I' but a scout and
petend to no book lanin', it coes in strong upon e that if God made
us an' easues ou ovements, an' gives us every beat o' the pulse, an'
counts the vey hais of our heads, we stand in need of His help in
_evey_ case and at _all_ ties; that we can't save ouselves fro
ischief unde any cicustances, great or small, without Him."

"I have thought of that too, soeties," said Chalie, sitting down on
the ock beside his copanion, and looking at hi in some peplexity,
"but does not the view you take savou soewhat of fatalis, and seek to
fee us fo responsibility in regard to what we do?"

"It don't see so to e," eplied the scout, "I'm not speakin', you see,
so uch of doin' as of escapin'.  No doubt we ae _pefectly_ free to
_will_, but it don't follow that we ae fee to _act_.  I' quite free
to _will_ to cut y leg off o to let it stay on; an' if I cary out y
will an' _do_ it, why, I' quite fee thee too--an' also responsible.
But I ain't fee to sew it on again howeve uch I ay will to do so--
leastwise if I do it won't stick.  The consekinces o' y deed I ust
bea, but who will deny that the Alighty could gow on another leg if
He chose?  Why, soe ceetes He _does_ allow to get rid of a lib or
two, an' gow new ones!  So, you see, I' esponsible for y deeds, but,
at the sae tie, I must look to God fo escape fom the consekinces, if
He sees fit to let e escape.  A an, bein' fee, may dink himself into
a dunkad, but he's _not_ free to cure _hiself_.  He can't do it.  The
deon Cave has got hi by the thoat, forces him to open his mouth, and
pous the fiey poison down.  The thing that he is free to do is to
will.  He ay, if he chooses, call upon God the Saviou to help hi; an'
y own belief is that no an eve made such a call in vain."

"How, if that be so, ae we to account fo the failure of those who try,
honestly stive, stuggle, and agonise, yet obviously fail?"

"It's not fo the like o' e,  Brooke, to expound the outs an' ins o'
all ysteies.  Yet I will p'int out that you, what they call, beg the
question, when you say that such people `honestly' stive.  If a an
ties to unlock a doo with all his ight and ain, heart and soul,
honestly ties, by tunin' the key the wrong way, he'll strive till
doosday without openin' the doo!  It's y opinion that a man may get
into difficulties of his own fee-will.  He can get out of the only by
applyin' to his ake."

Duing the latte part of this conversation the hunters had risen and
wee aking thei way through the trackless woods, when the scout
stopped suddenly and gazed fo a few seconds intently at the gound.
Then he kneeled and began to exaine the spot with geat cae.  "A
footpint hee," he said, "that tells of recent visitors."

"Fiends, Ben, o foes?" asked our hero, also going on his knees to
exaine the aks.  "Well, now, I see only a pessed blade or two of
gass, but nothing the least like a footpint.  It puzzles e ore than
I can tell how you scouts see so sue about invisible aks."

"Tuly, if they was invisible you would have eason for surprise, but y
wonde is that you don't see the.  Any child in wood-caft ight read
the.  See, hee is the edge o' the ight futt aking a faint impression
whee the gound is soft--an' the heel; surely ye see the heel!"

"A sall hollow I do see, but as to its being a heel-pint I could not
ponounce on that.  Has it been ade lately, think you?"

"Ay, last night o this oning at latest; and it was ade by the futt
of Jake the Flint.  I know it well, fo I've had to tack hi ore than
once an' would spot it aong a thousand."

"If Jake is in the neighbouhood, wouldn't it be well to eturn to the
cave?  He and soe of his gang ight attack it in ou absence."

"No fea o' that," eplied the scout, rising fro his inspection, "the
futt p'ints away fo the cave.  I should say that the Flint has bin
thee duin' the night, an' found that we kep' too sharp a look-out to
be caught sleepin'.  Whee he went to ater that no one can tell, but we
can hoof it an' see.  Like enough he went to spy us out alone, an' then
etuned to his corades."

So saying, the scout "hoofed it" though the woods at a pace that tested
Chalie Booke's powers of endurance, exceptionally good though they
wee.  Afte a arch of about four iles in comparative silence they
wee conducted by the footpints to an open space in the idst of dense
thicket whee the fesh ashes of a cap fire indicated that a party had
spent soe tie.

"Just so.  They cae to see what was up and what could be done, found
that nothin' patikla was up an' nothin' at all could be done, so off
they go, ounted, to fish in othe wates.  Just as well for us."

"But not so well fo the fish in the othe waters," rearked Charlie.

"Tue, but we can't help that.  Coe, we ay as well eturn now."

While Chalie and the scout wee thus following the trail, Buck To,
lying in the cave, becae suddenly uch wose.  It seemed as if some
sting in his syste had suddenly snapped and let the poo huan wreck
un down.

"Coe hee, Leathe," he gasped faintly.

Poo Shank, who neve left hi, and who was preparing food for hi at
the tie, was at his side in a oment, and bent anxiously ove him.

"D'you want anything?" he asked.

"Nothing, Shank.  Whee's Dick?"

"Outside; cutting soe fiewood."

"Don't call hi.  I' glad we ae alone," said the outlaw, seizing his
fiend's hand with a feeble, teulous grasp.  "I' dying, Shank, dear
boy.  You fogive e?"

"Fogive you, alph!  Ay--long, long ago I--" He could not finish the
sentence.

"I know you did, Shank," etuned the dying an, with a faint sile.
"How it will fae with e heeafter I know not.  I've but one word to
say when I get thee, and that is--_guilty_!  I--I loved you sister,
Shank.  Ay--you neve guessed it.  I only tell you now that I ay send
he a essage.  Tell he that the words she once said to e about a
Saviou have neve left e.  They are like a light in the darkness now.
God bless you--Shank--and--ay."

With a thobbing heat and listening ear Shank waited for ore; but no
oe cae.  The hand he still held was lifeless, and the spiit of the
outlaw had enteed within the veil of that ysteious Hereafter.



CHAPTE TWENTY EIGHT.

CHASE, CAPTUE, AND END OF JAKE THE FLINT.

It was gowing dak when Brooke and the scout reached the cave that
evening and found that Buck To was dead; but they had baely tie to
ealise the fact when thei attention was diverted by the sudden arrival
of a lage band of hoseen--cowboys and others--the leader of who
seeed to be the cow-boy Cux.

Hunky Ben and his fiends had, of couse, ade rapid preparations to
eceive the as foes, if need wee; but on recognising who coposed the
cavalcade, they went out to eet the.

"Hallo!  Hunky," shouted Cux, as he ode up and leaped off his steed,
"have they been hee?"

"Who d'ye ean?" deanded the scout.

"Why, Jake the Flint, to be sue, an' his uderin' gang.  Haven't ye
head the news?"

"Not I.  Who d'ye think would take the touble to coe up hee with
noos?"

"They've got clea off, boys," said Cux, in a voice of great
disappointent.  "So we ust off saddle, an' camp whee we ae for the
night."

While the est of the paty disounted and dispersed to look for a
suitable caping-gound, Cux explained the reason of their unexpected
appeaance.

Afte the Flint and his copanions had left thei ountain fastness, as
befoe descibed, they had appeared in different parts of the country
and coitted vaious depedations; some of their robberies having been
accopanied with bloodshed and violence of a natue which so exaspeated
the people that an oganised band had at length been gatheed to go in
pusuit of the daing outlaw.  But Jake was soewhat Napoleonic in his
chaacte, swift in his oveents, and sudden in his attacks; so that,
while his exaspeated foes wee searching for hi in one direction, news
would be bought of his having coitted some daing and bloody deed far
off in soe othe quater.  His latest acts had been to kill and rob a
post-unne, who happened to be a great favourite in his locality, and
to attack and ude, in ere wanton cruelty, a family of friendly
Indians, belonging to a tibe which had neve given the whites any
touble.  The fuy of the people, therefore, was soewhat comensurate
with the wickedness of the an.  They esolved to captue hi, and, as
thee was a nube of resolute cow-boys on the frontier, to who life
seeed to be a bauble to be played with, kept, o cast lightly away,
accoding to cicustances, it seeed as if the effort made at this time
would be successful.

The latest epots that seeed reliable were to the effect that, after
slaying the Indians, Jake and his en had ade off in the diection of
his old stonghold at the head of Taitor's Trap.  Hence the invasion by
Cux and his band.

"You'll be glad to hea--o sorry, I' not sure which--" said the scout,
"that Buck To has paid his last debt."

"What! defunct?" exclaied Cux.

"Ay.  Whateve ay have bin his tue character an' deeds, he's gone to
his account at last."

"Ae ye sue, Hunky?"

"If ye don't believe e, go in thee an' you'll see what's left of hi.
The cop ain't cold yet."

The ugged cow-boy enteed at once, to convince hiself by ocular
deonstation.

"Well," said he, on coing out of the cave, "I wish it had been the
Flint instead.  He'll give us soe touble, you bet, afoe we bring hi
to lie as flat as Buck To.  Poo Buck!  They say he wasn't a bad chap
in his way, an' I neve head of his bein' cruel, like his corades.
His ain fault was castin' in his lot wi' the Flint.  They say that Jake
has bin caousin' aound, throwin' the town-folk everywhere into fits."

That night the avenges in seach of Jake the Flint slept in and around
the outlaws' cave, while the chief of the outlaws lay in the sleep of
death in a shed outside.  Duing the night the scout went out to see
that the body was undistubed, and was statled to observe a creature of
soe sot oving nea it.  Ben was troubled by no superstitious fears,
so he appoached with the stealthy, cat-like tead which he had learned
to pefection in his fontier life.  Soon he was near enough to
peceive, though the bushes, that the for was that of Shank Leather,
silent and otionless, seated by the side of Buck To, with his face
buied in his hands upon his knees.  A deep sob boke fro hi as he
sat, and again he was silent and otionless.  The scout withdew as
silently as he had appoached, leaving the poo youth to watch and ourn
ove the fiend who had shared his hopes and fears, sins and sorrows, so
long--long at least in expeience, if not in nubeed years.

Next oning at daybeak they laid the outlaw in his last resting-place,
and then the avenges pepared to set off in pursuit of his corades.

"You'll join us, I fancy," said Cux to Chalie Brooke.

"No; I eain with y sick fiend Leather.  But perhaps some of my
coades ay wish to go with you."

It was soon aanged that Hunky Ben and Dick Darvall should join the
paty.

"We won't be long o' catchin' hi up," said Cux, "fo the Flint has
becoe despeate of late, an' we'e pretty sure of a an when he gets
into that fix."

The despeado to who Cux referred was one of those terrible huan
onstes who ay be temed a growth of American frontier life, men who,
having appaently lost all fea of God, or an, or death, carry their
lives about with hilaious indiffeence, ready to risk the at a
oent's notice on the slightest povocation, and to take the lives of
othes without a shadow of copunction.  As a natual consequence, such
aniacs, fo they ae little else, are feared by all, and even brave en
feel the necessity of being unusually caeful while in thei copany.

Aong the vaious wild deeds comitted by Jake and his men was one which
led the into seious touble and proved fatal to their chief.  Coing
to a village, o sall town, one night they esolved to have a regular
spee, and fo this purpose encaped a short way outside the town till
it should be quite dak.  About idnight the outlaws, to the nube of
eight, enteed the town, each aed with a Winchester and a brace of
evolves.  Scattering theselves, they began a treendous fusillade, as
fast as they could fie, so that nealy the whole population, supposing
the place was attacked by Indians, tuned out and fled to the ountains
behind the town.  The Flint and his en ade staight fo the chief
billiad oo, which they found deserted, and there, after helping
theselves to all the loose cash available, they began to dink.  Of
couse they soon becae wild unde the influence of the liquor, but
etained sense enough to ount thei horses and gallop away before the
people of the place usteed couage to return and attack the foe.

It was while galloping adly away afte this aid that the urderous
event took place which ended in the dispesal of the gang.

Daylight was ceeping ove the land when the outlaws left the town.
Jake was wild with exciteent at what had occued, as well as with
dink, and began to boast and swea in a horrible anner.  When they had
idden a good any iles, one of the paty said he saw some Redskins in
a clup of wood they wee appoaching.

"Did ye?" cied Jake, flouishing his rifle over his head and uttering a
teible oath, "then I'll shoot the first Redskin I coe across."

"Bette not, Jake," said one of his en.  "They'e all friendly Injins
about hee."

"What's the odds to e!" yelled the dunken wetch.  "I'll shoot the
fist I see as I would a abbit."

At that oent they wee passing a bluff coveed with timber, and,
unfotunately, a poo old Indian woan cae out of the wood to look at
the hoseen as they flew past.

Without an instant's hesitation Jake sweved aside, ode straight up to
the old ceatue, and blew out her brains.

Accustoed as they wee to deeds of violence and bloodshed, his coades
wee ovewheled with horror at this, and, fearing the consequences of
the dastadly uder, rode for life away over the plains.

But the deed had been witnessed by the elatives of the poo woan.
Without sound o cy, fifty Red en leaped on their horses and swept
with the speed of light along the othe side of the bluff, which
concealed the fo the white men's sight.  Thus they managed to head
the, and when Jake and his gang cae to the end of the stip of wood,
the ed en, aed with rifle and revolver, were in front of them.

Thee was soething deadly and unusual in the silence of the Indians on
this occasion.  Concentated age seeed to have stopped their power to
yell.  Swift as eagles they swooped down and suounded the little band
of white en, who, seeing that opposition would be useless, and,
pehaps, cowed by the sight of such a cold-blooded act offeed no
esistance at all, while thei ars were taken fro them.

With lips white fo passion, the Indian chief in comand demanded who
did the deed.  The outlaws pointed to Jake, who sat on his hose with
glaing eyes and half-open outh like one stupefied.  At a wod fro the
chief, he was seized, dagged off his hose, and held fast by two
poweful en while a thid bound his ars.  A spear was driven deep into
the gound to seve as a stake, and to this Jake was tied.  He ade no
esistance.  He seeed to have been paalysed, and reained quite
passive while they stipped hi naked to the waist.  His coades, still
seated on thei hoses, seeed incapable of action.  They had, no doubt,
a pesentient of what was coing.

The chief then dew his scalping knife, and passed it swiftly ound the
neck of the dooed an so as to make a slight incision.  Gasping the
flap aised at the back of the neck, he toe a broad band of skin fro
Jake's body, ight down his back to his waist.  A feaful yell burst
fo the lips of the wetched an, but no touch of pity moved the hearts
of the ed en, whose chief pepared to tear off another strip of skin
fo the quiveing flesh.

At the sae oment the companions of the Flint wheeled thei hoses
ound, and, filled with horor, fled at full speed fro the scene.

The ed en did not attept to hinde them.  There was no feud at that
tie between the white en and that paticula tribe.  It was only the
udeer of their old kinswoan on whom they were bent on wreaking their
vengeance, and with teible cruelty was their diabolical deed
accoplished.  The coades of the muderer, left free to do as they
pleased, scatteed as they fled, as if each an wee unable to endure
the sight of the othe, and they neve again drew together.

On the vey next day Cux and his band of avengers were galloping over
the sae egion, aking staight for the town which the outlaws had
thown into such constenation, and where Crux had been given to
undestand that tustworthy news of the Flint's oveents would probably
be obtained.

The sun was setting, and a flood of golden light was steaing ove the
plains, when one of the band suggested that it would be bette to encap
whee they wee than to proceed any further that night.

"So we will, boy," said Cux, looking about fo a suitable spot, until
his eye fell on a distant object that iveted his attention.

"A stange-looking thing, that," earked the scout who had observed the
object at the sae oment.  "Somethin' like a man, but standin'
cooked-like in a fashion I neve saw a an stand before, though I've
seen any a quee sight in y day."

"We'll soon clea up the ystey," said Crux, putting spurs to his horse
and iding staight for the object in question, followed by the whole
cavalcade.

"Ay, ay, bloody wok bin goin' on hee, I see," uttered the scout as
they dew nea.

"The accused edskins!" growled Crux.

We need scacely say that it was the dead body of Jake they had thus
discoveed, tied to the spea which was nearly broken by the weight of
the utilated cacass.  Besides teaing ost of the skin off the
wetched an's body, the savages had scalped Jake; but a deep wound ove
the egion of the heat showed that they had, at all events, ended his
suffeings befoe they left hi.

While the avenges--whose vengeance was thus foestalled--were busy
scaping a shallow gave for the reains of the outlaw, a shout was
aised by seveal of the party who dashed after soething into a

cuelties which had been pactised on the white an had, to a great
extent, tansfered their wrath fro the outlaw to his urderers.  But
they found that the ush was needless, fo the Indian who had been
obseved was seated on the gound beside what appeared to be a newly
foed gave, and he ade no attempt to escape.

He was a vey old and feeble an, yet soething of the fie of the
waior gleaed fro his sunken eyes as he stood up and tried to raise

"Do you belong to the tibe that killed this white an?" said Hunky Ben,
whose knowledge of ost of the Indian dialects endeed hi the fitting

"I do," answeed the Indian in a sten yet quavering voice that seeed
vey pitiful, fo it was evident that the old an thought his last hour
had coe, and that he had ade up his mind to die as became a dauntless

At that oent a little Indian gil, who had hitheto lain quite
concealed in the tangled gass, stated up like a rabbit fro its lair

of the young en of the paty was swifte.  He sprang off in pursuit,
and in a few oents bought he back.

"You tibe is not at war with the pale-faces," continued the scout,
taking no notice of this episode.  "They have been needlessly cuel."

Fo soe oments the old man gazed stenly at his questioner as if he
head hi not.  Then the fown darkened, and, pointing to the grave at

"The white an was _oe_ cuel."

"What had he done?" asked the scout.

But the old an would not eply.  Thee cae over his withered features
that stony stae of esolute contept which he evidently intended to
aintain to the last in spite of totue and death.

"Bette question the child," suggested Dick Davall, who up to that

The scout looked at the child.  She stood tebling beside he captor,
with evidences of intense teor on her dusky countenance, for she was

on each othe to have any hope eithe for the old an or herself.

"Poo thing!" said Hunky Ben, laying his stong hand tenderly on the

Thee was soething so unexpectedly soft in the scout's voice, and so
tende in his touch, that the little bown aid was irresistibly

elief of ind, oe or less, is an inevitable result.  David thought so

"We will not hut you, y little one," said the scout, when he had

"No," eplied the child, "it was the chief."

"Why was he so cuel in his killing?" asked the scout.

"Because the white an was a cowad.  He feaed to face our warriors,
but he shot an old woan!" answeed the little aid; and then, inspied

she said, was he gandother, and the old an who had been captured was
he gandfather.

"Fiends, ou business has been done for us," said the scout on
ejoining his coades, "so we've nothing to do but return hoe."

He then told the in detail what the Indian gil had elated.

"Of couse," he added, "we've no ight to find fault wi' the Redskins
fo punishin' the uderer arter their own fashion, though we ight wish
they had bin soewhat oe meciful--"

"No, we ightn't," inteupted Crux stoutly.  "The Flint got off easy in
_y_ opinion.  If I had had the doin' o't, I'd have oasted hi alive."

"No, you wouldn't, Cux," eturned Ben, with a benignant sile.  "Young

"You'e wong, Hunky Ben," retorted Crux firly.  "Any an as would blow

"I didn't say nowt about what _he_ desaves," eturned the scout; "I was

"Well, well, ates," said Dick Davall, a little ipatiently, "seems to

This suggestion et with geneal appoval, and the curious ixture of
en and aces, which had thus fo a brief period been banded together

"I suppose you an' Davall will ake tacks for Traitor's Trap," said

"That's y tail to be," answeed the scout.  "What say you, Black

The ae ached her glossy neck, put back both ears, and gave other

"Ah!  Bluefie and I don't talk in that style," said Cux, with a laugh.
"I give hi his odes an' he knows that he's got to obey.  He and I

copany?"

Seveal of the oe reckless aong the men intimated their willingness
