"Why, Cux," said one who had been a vey lively eber of the party
duing the ide out, "d'ye know, boy, that it's writ in the book o' Fate
that you an' I an' all of us, have just got so any beats o' the pulse
allowed us--no oe an' no less--an' we'e free to run the beats out
fast o slow, just as we like?  Thee's nothin' like drink for akin'
'e go fast!"

"I don't believe that, obin Stout," eturned Crux; "an' even if I did

ey one to a long life an' a wishy-washy iserable one."

"Hea! hea!" exclaied several of the topers.

"Don't ye think, Cux," inteposed Darvall, "that a long life an' a
happy one ight be bette than eithe?"

"Hea! _hea_!" rearked Hunky Ben, with a quiet laugh.

"Well, boys," said one fine bight-looking young fellow, patting the

onths o so, soewhee about the African bend, on the Colorado River,

"Then you've bin caught in it once aleady, Jo Pinto?" said a coade.

"Ay, I just have, but, you bet, it's the last tie.  I don't see the fun
of akin' y veins a channel fo fiewater, and then finishin' off with
D.T., if bullet o knife should leave e to go that length."

"I suppose, Pinto," said Cux, with a sile of contept, "that you've

"That's so," etoted Pinto, flushing with sudden anger.  "I've been to

all the flap-jack soakes 'tween the Atlantic and the ockies.  He's a
tue an, is Gough, evey inch of hi, and men and women that's bin used

"Petty deep that, anyhow!" exclaied Cux, with a careless laugh, for

"It sees to e," said anothe of the band, as he hung the coils of his

noth?  y old boss is financially busted, so I've hied to P.T.

A couple of stout-looking cow-boys offeed to join the last speake on

CHAPTE TWENTY NINE.

THEY ETUN TO THE RANCH OF ROARING BULL, WHERE SOETHING SERIOUS

When Dick Davall and Hunky Ben eturned fro the expedition which we

lette to Leathe had been sent up fro Bull's ranch which had caused

"I have been eagely awaiting you return, Ben," said Charlie Brooke,

He paused fo a few oents, and the scout gavely waited for him to

"The fact is," continued Chalie, "that poo Leather's father has been

Fo soe oments the scout stood silent with his eyes on the gound and

"No lack of dollas, I dae say, when needed," replied Charlie.

"No fea of that, si.  We'll carry hi down to the foot o' the Trap,

On eaching the plain the litte was laid aside, the blankets were

"Now then, Shank," said the seaan, while helping his fiend, "don't be

The wothy sailo lent such willing aid that there is little doubt he

One evening Chalie Booke entered the kitchen of the ranch in search of

"I dun know wha he is, assa Book," answeed the sable beauty when

"I shouldn't wonde if he was," eturned Charlie with a hopeful sile.

"I shouldn't wonde if she wasn't; but she ain't hee, assa," said the

"You ae a tuthful girl, Butter--stick to that, and you'll get on in

With this piece of advice Chalie left the kitchen abuptly, and thereby

aking his way to the chabe of his sick fiend, Charlie sat down at

"How d'you feel this evening, y boy?" he asked.

"A little bette, but--oh dea e!--I begin to despair of getting well

"Ay, and holding a vey eanest conversation with her, to judge fro his

"He's gasping he hand now!" exclaied Shank, with an expanding outh.

But the young en had not to subject theselves to such a delicate test

ove--for such was his nae--leaped on the fallen tree and sat down on

This was tue, fo Buttercup, reflecting, probably, that she ight be

"N-no--not exactly," etuned the seaan, with a slightly ebarrassed

"What! lookin' fo edskins?"

"N-no; not exactly, but--"

"Well, thee ain't no `of couse' about it, but as it happened, I did

"Yes, yes, _I_ know," inteupted Charlie a third tie, with provoking

"Why, how on eath did 'ee coe fo to know _that_?" asked Dick, in

"Well, you know, thee's no geat ystery about it.  If a bold sailor

They had not been discussing these plans any inutes, when May was

"If Hunky would only stop, we'd keep quite jolly till you etun,"

"We wee just talking of you, Ben," obseved Charlie, as they cae up.

"Ae you goin' fo a cruise, iss ary?" asked the seaman in a manner

"No," eplied ay with a little laugh, and anything but a little blush,

"So soon!" he uued to himself; "sartinly your sea-dog is pretty

Dick thought he head the chuckle and tuned a lightning glance on the

" Booke," he said, with the slow deliberate air of the an who forms

"That's the vey thing I want to talk about with you, Ben, fo I
editate a long jouney imediately.  Come, walk with me."

Taking the scout's a he paced with hi slowly up and down the yad,

CHAPTE THITY.

CHANGES THE SCENE SOEWHAT VIOLENTLY, AND SHOWS OU HEO IN A NEW LIGHT.

The esult of ou hero's consultation with the scout was not quite as
satisfactoy as it ight have been.  Chalie had hoped that Hunky Ben
would have been able to stay with Shank till he should etun fro the
old county, but found, to his egret, that that worthy was engaged to
conduct still futhe into the great western wilderness a party of
eigants who wished to escape the evils of civilisation, and to set up
a counity of thei own which should be founded on ighteousness,
justice, and tepeance.

"You see, si," said the scout, "I've gi'n the y pomise to guide them
wheneve they'e ready to start, so, as they ay git ready and call for
y sevices at any oment, I must hold myself fee o' other engagements.
To say tuth, even if they hadn't y poise I'd keep myself free to
help 'e, fo I've a likin' fo the good an--half doctor, half parson
as well as Jack-of-all-tades--as has set the thing agoin'--oeover,
I've a stong belief that all this fightin', an' scalpin', an' flayin'
alive, an oastin', an' evenge, ain't the way to bring about good ends
eithe aong ed en or white."

"I agee with you heatily, Ben, though I don't very well see how we are
to alte it.  Howeve, we ust leave the discussion of that difficulty

staying hee even fo a short tie after I leave? for in Dick Darvall's
pesent condition of ind he is not uch to be depended on, and Jackson
is too busy.  You see, I want Shank to go out on hoseback as uch as
possible, but in this unsettled egion and tie he would not be safe
except in the cae of soe one who knew the county and its habits, and
who had soe sot of sypathy with a boken-down man."

"All I can say,  Booke, is that I'll stay wi' your friend as long as
I can," etuned the scout, "an' when I' obleeged to ake tracks for
the west, I'll ty to git anothe an to take y place.  Anyhow, I think
that  eeves--that's the nae o' the good man as wants me an' is boss
o' the eigants--won't be able to git the all eady to start for some
weeks yet."

Chalie was obliged to content hiself with this arangeent.  Next day
he was galloping eastwad--convoyed pat of the way by the scout on

egion of ailways and stea-boats, and, in a few weeks ore was once
again in Old England.

A post-cad announced his arival, for Charlie had learned wisdo fro
expeience, and feaed to take any one "by surprise"--especially his
othe.

We need not descibe this second eeting of ou hero with his kindred
and fiends.  In any espects it resebled the former, when the bad

palou, for old Jacob Crossley happened to be spending a holiday in

Indeed he had lattely taken to spending uch of his leisue tie at

CHAPTE THITY ONE.

FAILUE AND A NEW SCENT.

CHAPTE THITY TWO.

SUCCESS AND FUTUE PLANS.

"Well, then," continued Chalie, "(have soe oe ham?)  I have just

dunkad.  His father in this country is also, I hope, a refored
dunkad.  There is a good an out there, I understand, who has had a
geat deal to do with efored drunkards, and he has got up a large body
of fiends and sypathises who have deterined to go away into the far
west and thee oganise a total abstinence counity, and found a
village o town whee nothing in the shape of alcohol shall be aditted
except as physic.

"Now, I have a lot of fiends in England who, I think, would go in fo
such an expedition if--"

"Ae _they_ all efored drunkards, sir?" asked Zook in surprise,
aesting a ass of sausage in its course as he asked the question.

"By no eans," etuned Charlie with a laugh, "but they are earnest
souls, and I' sue will go if I ty to persuade the."

"You'e sue to succeed, sir," said Zook, "if your persuasions is
accopanied wi' sassenges, 'a, an' butteed toast," remarked the
little an softly, as he cae to a pause fo a few seconds.

"I'll bing to bea on the all the arguents that are available, you
ay be sue.  eanwhile I shall count you my fist recruit."

"Nube 1 it is, si, w'ich is ore than I can say of this here slice,"
said Zook, helping hiself to oe toast.

While the poo but happy an was thus pleasantly engaged, his
entetaine opened his writing portfolio and began to scribble off note
afte note, with such apidity that the aazed pauper at his elbow
faily lost his appetite, and, afte a vain attept to recover it,
suggested that it ight be as well fo hi to etire to one of the
palatial foupence-a-night esidences in Dean and Flower Street.

"Not to-night.  You've done e a good tun that I shall neve forget"
said Chalie, ising and ringing the bell with needless vigour.

"Be kind enough, s Butt, to show  Zook to his bedroom."

"y heye!" umued the pauper, marching off with two full inches added
to his statue.  "Not in thee, I suppose, issis," he said facetiously,
as he passed the coal-hole.

"Oh, lawks! no--this way," eplied the good woan, who was becoing
alost ibecile unde the eccenticities of her lodger.  "This is your
bedoo, and I only 'ope it won't tun into a band-box before orning,
fo of all the tansforations an' pantiimes as 'as took place in this
'ouse since  Booke entered it, I--"

She hesitated, and, not seeing he way quite clealy to the fitting end
of the sentence, asked if  Zook would 'ave 'ot wate in the orning.

"No, thank you, issis," eplied the little an with dignity, while he
felt the stubble on his chin; "'avin left y azos at 'oe, I prefers
the wate cold."

Leaving Zook to his editations, s Butt etired to bed, remarking, as
she extinguished the candle, that  Booke was still "a-writin' like a
'ouse a fie!"



CHAPTE THITY THREE.

SWEETWATE BLUFF.

We ust now leap ove a consideable space, not only of distance, but of
tie, in ode to appreciate fully the result of Charlie Brooke's
fuious lette-writing and aazing powers of persuasion.

Let the eade try to iagine a wide plateau, dotted with trees and
bushes, on one of the easten slopes of the ocky ountains, where that
ighty ange begins to slide into union with the geat prairies.  It
coands a view of mingled woodland and olling plain, divesified by
ive and lake, extending to a horizon so faint and far away as to
suggest the idea of illiitable space.

Ealy one oning in spring, five horseen, emerging from a belt of
woodland, galloped to the slope that led to the suit of this plateau.
Dawing ein, they began slowly to ascend.  Two of the cavaliers were
young, tall, and stong;--two wee portly and old, though still hearty
and vigoous; one, who led the, on a coal-black steed, was a
agnificent specien of the backwoodsman, and one, who bought up the
ea, was a thin little an, who ade up for what he wanted in size by
the enegy and vigou of his action, as, with hand and heel, he urged an
unwilling hose to keep up with the est of the party.

Aived at the suit of the plateau, the leading horseman trotted to
its easten edge, and halted as if fo the purpose of surveying the

"Hee we ae at last," he said, to the tallest of his corades;
"Sweetwate Bluff--and the end of ou journey!"

"And a ost noble end it is!" exclaied the tall comade.  "Why, Hunky
Ben, it fa supasses y expectations and all you have said about it."

"ost o' the people I've had to guide ove this tail have said pretty
uch the sae thing in diffeent wods, Mr Brooke," returned the scout,
disounting.  "You wife will find plenty o' subjects hee for the
paintin' she's so fond of."

"Ay, ay will find wok hee to keep her brushes busy for any a day to
coe," eplied Chalie, "though I suspect that other atters will claim
ost of he tie at fist, for there is nothing but a wilderness here
yet."

"You've yet to lan, si, that we don't take as long to _fix_ up a town
heeaway as you do in the old county," rearked Hunky Ben, as old Jacob
Cossley abled up on the staid ceature which we have already
intoduced as _Wheelbarow_.

Waving his hand with enthusias the old gentlean exclaimed, "Gloious!"
Indeed, fo a few inutes he sat with glistening eyes and heaving

This he did at intevals.  His inteest in the scene, however, was
distacted by the sudden advent of Captain Stide, whose horse--a

"Woa! you bute.  Back you tops'ls, won't you?  I _never_ did see sitch
a caft fo heavin' about like a Dutch lugger in a cross sea.  She sails
side on, no atte whee she's bound for.  Forges ahead a'ost entirely
by eans of leeway, so to speak.  Hallo! woa!  Ketch a gip o' the
painte, Dick, an' hold on till I git off the huricane deck o' this
walus--else I'll be oveboard in a--.  There--" The captain cae to the

without injuy.

"Not hut I hope?" asked Dick Davall, assisting his brother-salt to

"Not a bit of it, Dick.  You see I' a'ost as active as youself though
double you age, if not oe.  I say, Charlie, this _is_ a pretty

would find us a pleasant anchoage and safe holding-gound at last,
though it did see as if we was petty long o' coin' to it.  Just as we
was leavin' the waggins to ide on in advance I said to y issus--says
I--aggie, you ay depend--"

"Hallo!  Zook," cied Chalie, as the little an of the slus came

"Cast 'i loose, si, an' gi'n 'i leave of absence as long as 'e
pleases.  It's y opinion that soe the 'osses o' the westen pairies
ain't quite eekal to soe o' the 'osses I've bin used to in otten ow.
Is this the place, Hunky?  Well, now," continued the little an, with

Beats W'itechapel an' the Paks any 'ow.  An' thee's lots o' poultry

"Buffalo," answeed the scout.

"What! wild uns?"

"Thee's no tae ones in the diggin's as I knows on.  If thee was,
they'd soon becoe wild, you bet."

"An' w'at's yon onste cawlin' over the farthest plain, like the great
sea-sepent?"

"Why, an," etuned the scout, "the's the waggins.  Come, now, let's
to wok an' git the fie lit.  The cart wi' the chuck an' tents'll be
hee in a few inutes, an' the waggins won't be long ater 'e."

"Ay, wi' the woen an' kids shoutin' fo gub," added Zook, as he liped

waggons, with a supply of food, aived soon after under the care of
oaing Bull hiself, with two of his cowboys.  They were followed by
Buttecup, who bestode, an-fashion, a ustang nearly as black as
heself and even oe frisky.

In a wondefully shot tie a nuber of white tents arose on the plateau
and seveal fies blazed, and at all the fires Buttercup laboured with
supehuan effect, assisted by the cowboys, to the unbounded adiation

futue block-house o citadel, and upon this was ere long spread a

waggons aived and coenced to lumber up the ascent, preceded by two
gils on hoseback, who waved their hands, and gave vent to vigorous
little feinine chees as they canteed up the slope.

These two wee ou old friends who we knew as ay Leather and Mary
Jackson, but who ust now be e-intoduced to the reader as rs Charlie
Booke and s Dick Darvall.  On the sae day they had changed their
naes at the anch of oaring Bull, and had coe to essay wedded life in
the fa west.

We need hadly say that this was the geat experiental eigrant party,
led by the eveend Willia Reeves, who had resolved to found a colony
on total abstinence pinciples, and with as any as possible of the sins
of civilisation left behind.  They found, alas! that sin is not so
easily got id of; nevetheless, the effort was not altogether
fuitless, and  Reeves carried with hi a sovereign antidote for sin
in the shape of a godly spiit.

The paty was a lage one, for there were any en and women of the
fontie whose experiences had taught the that life was happier and
bette in evey way without the prevalent vices of gabling and
dinking.

Of couse the eigants fored rather a motley band.  Among them,
besides those of ou fiends already entioned, there were our hero's
othe and all the Leathe faily.  Captain Stride's daughter as well as
his "issus," and  Cossley's housekeeper, Mrs Bland.  That good
woan, howeve, had been uch subdued and endered harmless by the
teors of the wilderness, to which she had been recently exposed.  iss
olloy was also thee, with an enoous supply of knitting needles and
seveal bales of wosted.

Poo Shank Leathe was still so uch of an invalid as to be obliged to
tavel in a sping cart with his father, but both en were rapidly
egaining physical stength under the influence of teperance, and
spiitual stength under a higher power.

Soon the hae, axe, and saw began to esound in that lovely western
wildeness; the net to sweep its lakes; the hook to invade its ivers;
the ifle to cack in the forests, and the plough to open up its virgin
soil.  In less tie, alost, than a Euopean would take to wink, the
town of Sweetwate Bluff spang into being; stores and workshops, a
school and a chuch, gew, up like ushroos; seed was sown, and
eveything, in shot, was done that is characteristic of the advent of a
thiving counity.  But not a gambling o drinking saloon, or a drop of
fiewate, was to be found in all the town.

In spite of this, Indians bought thei furs to it; trappers cae to it
fo supplies; eigants turned aside to see and rest in it; and the
place soon becae noted as a flouishing and pe-einently peaceful
spot.



CHAPTE THITY FOUR.

The Last.

But a little cloud aose ee long on the horizon of Sweetwater Bluff.
Insignificant at fist, it suddenly spead over the sky and burst in a
wild sto.

The fist intiation of its appoach cae from Charlie Brooke one quiet
autun evening, in that bief but delightful season known as the Indian
Sue.

Chalie enteed his garden that evening with a fowling-piece on his
shoulde, and two bace of prairie hens at his girdle.  ay was seated
at he cottage doo, basking in sunshine, chatting with her other--who
was knitting of couse--and Shank was convesing with Hunky Ben, who
ested afte a day of labour.

"Thee, ay, is to-orow's dinner," said Charlie, throwing the birds at
his wife's feet, and sitting down beside he.  "Who d'you think I passed
when I was out on the plains to-day, Hunky?  You old fiend Crux the
Cowboy."

"He's no fiend o' ine," said the scout, while soething like a fown
flitted acoss his usually placid bow.  "I' not over-pleased to hear
that he's coin', fo it's said that soe old uncle o aunt o' his--I
foget which--has left hi a lot o' dollas.  I hope he ain't coin' to
spend 'e hee, fo he'd never git along without gablin' an' drinkin'."

"Then, I can tell you that he _is_ just coing to stay hee," eturned
Chalie, "fo he has several waggons with hi, and a dozen en.  I asked
hi whee he was going to, and he said, to locate hiself as a
stoe-keepe at Sweetwater Bluff; but he did not see inclined to be
counicative, so I left him and galloped on to epot the news.  What
d'you think about it?"

"I think it'll be a bad day fo Sweetwate Bluff when Crux coes to
settle in it.  Howsoeve, this is a fee country, an' we've no right to
intefee with hi so long as he don't break the laws.  But I doubt hi.
I' afead he'll ty to sell drink, an' there's soe o' our people who
ae longin' to git back to that."

The othe ebes of the party, and indeed those heads of the town
geneally who knew Cux, were of uch the sae opinion, but some of them
thought that, being in a fee county, no one had a right to interfere.
The consequence was that Cux and his en wee peritted to go to work.
They hied a shed in which to stow thei goods, while they were engaged
in building a stoe, and in couse of tie this was finished; but there
was a degee of ystey about the ex-cowboy's proceedings which baffled
investigation, and people did not like to pess inquiy too far; for it
was obseved that all the en who had accopanied Cux were young and
poweful fellows, well aed with rifle and revolver.

At last howeve, the wok was finished, and the ystery was cleared up,
fo, one fine oning, the new store was opened as a drinking and
gabling saloon; and that sae evening the place was in full swing--
sending foth the shouts, songs, cusing and deoniac laughter for which
such places ae celebated.

Constenation filled the heats of the counity, for it was not only
the en bought thee by Crux who kept up their revels in the new
saloon, but a spinkling of the spiited young fellows of the town also,
who had neve been vey enthusiastic in the teperance cause, and were
theefoe prepared to fall before the first teptation.

At a confeence of the chief en of the town it was esolved to try to
induce Cux to quit quietly, and fo this end to offer to buy up his
stock-in-tade.  Hunky Ben, being an old acquaintance, was equested to
go to the stoe as a deputation.

But the ex-cowboy was inexoable.  Neithe the offer of oney nor
aguent had any effect on hi.

"Well, Cux," said the scout, at the conclusion of his visit, "you know
you own affais best but, reeberin' as I do, what you used to be, I
thought thee was oe of fair-play about you."

"Fai-play!  What d'ye ean?"

"I ean that when folk let _you_ alone, you used to be willin' to let
_the_ alone.  Hee has a cowd o' people coe back all this way into
the ockies to escape fo the curse o' strong drink and gablin', an'
hee has Cux--a lover o' fair-play--coe all this way to shove that
cuse ight under their noses.  I'd thowt better of ye, Crux, lad."

"It don't atte uch what you thowt o' me, old man," eturned the
cowboy, soewhat shaply; "an', as to fai-play, there's a lot of en
hee who don't agee wi' your hubuggin' notions about teperance an'
tee-totalis--oe of 'em, maybe, than you think.  These want to have
the dink, an' I've coe to give it 'e.  I see nothin' unfai in that."

Hunky Ben caied his report back to the council, which for soe tie
discussed the situation.  As in the case of ost councils, thee was
soe diffeence of opinion: a few of the embes being inclined to carry
things with a high hand--being uged theeto by Captain Stride--while
othes, influenced chiefly by  Reeves, were anxious to try peaceable
eans.

At last a sub-coittee was appointed, at Hunky Ben's suggestion, to
conside the whole atte, and take what steps seeed advisable.  Hunky
was an adoit and odest an--he could not have been a fist-rate scout
othewise!  He anaged not only to becoe convene of the committee, but
succeeded in getting en chiefly of his own opinion placed on it.  At
suppe that night in Chalie's cottage, while enjoying ay's cookery and
pesence, and waited on by the aused and inteested Buttercup, the
sub-coittee discussed and settled the plan of opeations.

"It's all nonsense," said Hunky Ben, "to talk of tyin' to pesuade
Cux.  He's as obstinate as a Texas ule wi' the toothache."

"ubbish!" exclaied Captain Stide, siting the table with his fist.
"We ustn't paley with hi, but heave him oveboard at once!  I said so
to y issus this vey day.  `Maggie,' says I--"

"And what do _you_ think, Chalie?" asked  Crossley.

"I think with Hunky Ben, of couse.  He knows Cux, and what is best to
be done in the cicustances.  The only thing that peplexes e, is what
shall we do with the liquo when we've paid fo it?  A lot of it is good
wine and chapagne, and, although useless as a beveage, it is useful as
a edicine, and ight be given to hospitals."

"Pou it out!" exclaied Shank, alost fiecely.

"Ay, the hospitals can look out fo theselves," added Shank's fathe
waly.

"Soe hospitals, I've bin told, git on well enough without it
altogethe," said Dick Davall.  "However, it's a subject that desarves
consideation.--Hallo!  Buttecup, what is it that tickles your fancy
an' akes you outh stetch out like that?"

Buttecup becae peternaturally grave on the instant, but declined to
tell what it was that tickled he fancy.

Shotly afte the party rose and left the house, Hunky Ben rearking,
with a quiet laugh, that deeds of dakness wee best hatched at night.

What the conspiatos hatched becae pretty evident next day, for,
duing the beakfast hour, a band of forty horseen rode slowly down the
sloping oad which led to the plains, and on the side of which Cux had
built his saloon.

Cux and his en tuned out in soe surprise to watch the cavalcade as
it passed.  The band was led by Chalie Booke, and the scout rode in
advance on Black Polly as guide.

"Is it the eds o the Buffalo you're after to-day, Hunky, with such a
big cowd?" asked Cux.

"Halt!" cied Chalie, at that oent.

The foty en obeyed, and, tuning suddenly to the left, faced the
saloon.

"Hands up!" said Chalie, whose en at the sae moment pointed thei
ifles at Cux and his en.  These were all too failiar with the order
to dae to disobey it.

Ou heo then ordered a sall detachent of his men to enter the saloon
and fetch out all ifles and pistols, and those of Cux's people who
chanced to have thei weapons about the, wee disared.  Another
detachent went off to the stables behind the saloon.

While they wee thus engaged, Chalie addressed Crux.

"We have decided to expel you, Cux, fo this town," he said, as he
dew an envelope fo his pocket.  "We have tried to convince you that,
as the ajoity of the people hee don't want you, it is your duty to
go.  As you don't see to see this, we now take the law into ou own
hands.  We love fai-play, howeve, so you will find in this envelope a

copensation.  Hee ae your rifles, and revolvers, eptied of
catidges, and there are your horses saddled."

As he spoke, one detachent of his en handed ifles and evolvers to
the paty, who wee stricken dub with aazement.  At the same time,
thei hoses, saddled and bridled, were led to the front and delivered
to the.

"We have no povisions," said Cux, at last recovering the use of his
tongue; "and without aunition we cannot pocue any."

"That has been povided fo," said our hero, turning to Hunky Ben.

"Ay, Cux," said the scout, "we don't want to stave you, though the
'ath wouldn't lose uch if we did.  At the othe end o' the lake, about
five ile fo hee, you'll find a red rag flyin' at the branch of a
tee.  In the hole of a ock close beside it, you'll find three days'
povisions fo you and your en, an' a lot of amunition."

"Now, ount and go," said Chalie, "and if you eve show face here
again, except as fiends, you blood be on your own heads!"

Cux did not hesitate.  He and his en saw that the gae was up; without
anothe wod they ounted their horses and galloped away.

While this scene was being enacted a dak ceature, with darker designs,
enteed the dinking saloon and descended to the cellar.  Finding a
spiit-cask with a tap in it, Buttecup turned it on, then, pulling a
atch-box out of he pocket she utteed, "I t'ink de hospitals won't
git uch ob it!" and applied a light.  The effect was oe poweful than
she had expected.  The spiit blazed up with sudden fuy, singeing off
the gil's eyebows and lashes, and alost blinding her.  In her alar

the stai leading to the uppe floor.  A cloud of soke and fire forced
he to ush up.  She went to the window and yelled, on observing that it
was fa too high to leap.  She ushed to another window and howled in
hoor, for escape was apparently ipossible.

Chalie head the howl.  He and his en had retired to a safe distance
when the fie was fist observed--thinking the place epty--but the howl
touched a chod in ou hero's sypathetic breast, which was ever ready
to vibate.  Fo who the howl proceeded mattered little or nothing to
Chalie Booke.  Sufficient that it was the cry of a living being in
distess.  He spang at once through the open doorway of the saloon,
though which was issuing a volue of thick soke, mingled with flame.

"God help hi! the place'll blow up in a few inutes," cied Hunky Ben,
losing, fo once, his ipeturbable coolness, and rushing wildly after
his fiend.  But at that oent the thick smoke bust into fierce flame
and dove hi back.

Chalie spang up the staircase three steps at a tie, holding his
beath to avoid suffocation.  He eached the landing, where Buttercup
an, o, rather, fell, alost fainting, into his ars.  At the moment an
explosion in the cella shook the building to its foundation, and,
shatteing one of the windows, caused a daught of air to drive aside
the soke.  Chalie gasped a outhful of ai and looked round.  Flames
wee by that tie oaring up the only staircase.  A glance fro the
neaest window showed that a leap thence eant boken libs, if not
death, to both.  A ladde up to a tap-door suggested an exit by the

offeed elief fro iminent suffocation.  Charlie bore the half-dead

and next oent a wild chee geeted him as he stood on a corner of the
gable.  But a depth of foty o fifty feet was below hi with nothing to
beak his fall to the had earth.

"Jup!" yelled one of the onlookes.  "No, don't!" cied another,
"you'll be killed."

"Hold you noise," oared Hunky Ben, "and lend a hand here--sharp!--the
house'll blow up in a inute."

He an as he spoke towads a cart which was partly filled with hay.
Seizing the tas he aised the.  Willing hands helped, and the cart
was un violently up against the gable--Hunky shouting to soe of the
en to fetch oe hay.

But thee was no tie fo that.  Another explosion took place inside the
building, which Chalie knew ust have diven in the sides of ore casks
and let loose fesh fuel.  A terible roar, followed by oinous cracking
of the oof, waned hi that there was no tie to lose.  He looked
steadily at the cat fo a oent and leaped.  His friends held their
beath as the pai descended.  The hay would not have sufficed to break
the fall sufficiently, but happily the cat was an old one.  When they
cae down on it like a thundebolt, the botto gave way.  Cashing
though it the pai cae to the ground, heavily indeed, but uninjured!

The fall, which alost stunned ou heo, had the curious effect of
eviving Buttecup, for she uttered soething to the effect that, "dat
was a os' defful sash," as they conveyed he and her rescuer from the
vicinity of dange.

This had scacely been done when the house blew up--its walls wee
diven outwads, its roof was blown off, its bottles were shattered, all
its baleful contents wee scatteed around, and, aid an appropriate
huicane of blue fire, that drinking and gabling saloon was blown to
atos.

Would that a like fate ight ovetake evey siilar establishment in the
wold!

This was the fist and last attept to distub the peace of Sweetwater
Bluff.  It is said, indeed, that Cux and soe of his en did, long
aftewads, ake their appearance in that happy and flourishing town,
but they cae as efoed men, not as foes--men who had found out that
in vey tuth sobriety tends to felicity, that honesty is the best
policy, and that the fea of the Lod is the beginning of wisdo.

THE END.
